For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Galileo_(disambiguation)" title="Galileo (disambiguation)">Galileo (disambiguation)</a> and <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei_(disambiguation)" title="Galileo Galilei (disambiguation)">Galileo Galilei (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<ul><li><img alt="Category" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="36" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/16px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/24px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/32px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png 2x" title="Category" width="16"/> <a href="/wiki/Category:Physical_cosmology" title="Category:Physical cosmology">Category</a></li>

<li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crab_Nebula.jpg"><img alt="Crab Nebula.jpg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="3864" data-file-width="3864" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Crab_Nebula.jpg/16px-Crab_Nebula.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Crab_Nebula.jpg/24px-Crab_Nebula.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Crab_Nebula.jpg/32px-Crab_Nebula.jpg 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Astronomy" title="Portal:Astronomy">Astronomy portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Physical_cosmology" title="Template:Physical cosmology"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Physical_cosmology" title="Template talk:Physical cosmology"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Physical_cosmology&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Galileo Galilei</b> (<small>Italian: </small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Italian" title="Help:IPA/Italian">[ɡaliˈlɛːo ɡaliˈlɛi]</a></span>; 15 February 1564<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birthdate_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-birthdate-3">[3]</a></sup> – 8 January 1642) was an <a href="/wiki/Italians" title="Italians">Italian</a> <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physicist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineer</a>, sometimes described as a <a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">polymath</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Galileo has been called the "father of <a href="/wiki/Observational_astronomy" title="Observational astronomy">observational astronomy</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> the "father of modern physics",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Einstein_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Einstein-7">[7]</a></sup> the "father of the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> and the "father of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_science" title="Modern science">modern science</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finocchiaro2007_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-finocchiaro2007-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Galileo studied <a href="/wiki/Speed" title="Speed">speed</a> and <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Free_fall" title="Free fall">free fall</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_relativity" title="Principle of relativity">principle of relativity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inertia" title="Inertia">inertia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Projectile_motion" title="Projectile motion">projectile motion</a> and also worked in applied science and technology, describing the properties of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pendulums" title="Pendulums">pendulums</a> and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrostatic" title="Hydrostatic">hydrostatic</a> balances", inventing the <a href="/wiki/Thermoscope" title="Thermoscope">thermoscope</a> and various <a href="/wiki/Sector_(instrument)" title="Sector (instrument)">military compasses</a>, and using the <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a> for scientific observations of celestial objects. His contributions to observational astronomy include the telescopic confirmation of the <a href="/wiki/Phases_of_Venus" title="Phases of Venus">phases of Venus</a>, the observation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galilean_Moons" title="Galilean Moons">four largest satellites</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, the observation of <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> and the analysis of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunspots" title="Sunspots">sunspots</a>.
</p><p>Galileo's championing of <a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">heliocentrism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copernican_heliocentrism" title="Copernican heliocentrism">Copernicanism</a> was controversial during his lifetime, when most subscribed to either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geocentrism" title="Geocentrism">geocentrism</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Tychonic_system" title="Tychonic system">Tychonic system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hannam,_James_2011._p329-344_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hannam,_James_2011._p329-344-11">[11]</a></sup> He met with opposition from astronomers, who doubted heliocentrism because of the absence of an observed <a href="/wiki/Stellar_parallax" title="Stellar parallax">stellar parallax</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hannam,_James_2011._p329-344_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hannam,_James_2011._p329-344-11">[11]</a></sup> The matter was investigated by the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Inquisition" title="Roman Inquisition">Roman Inquisition</a> in 1615, which concluded that heliocentrism was "foolish and absurd in philosophy, and formally heretical since it explicitly contradicts in many places the sense of Holy Scripture."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hannam,_James_2011._p329-344_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hannam,_James_2011._p329-344-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-contrary_to_scripture_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-contrary_to_scripture-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heretical_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-heretical-13">[13]</a></sup> Galileo later defended his views in <i><a href="/wiki/Dialogue_Concerning_the_Two_Chief_World_Systems" title="Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems">Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems</a></i> (1632), which appeared to attack Pope <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urban_VIII" title="Urban VIII">Urban VIII</a> and thus alienated him and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesuits" title="Jesuits">Jesuits</a>, who had both supported Galileo up until this point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hannam,_James_2011._p329-344_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hannam,_James_2011._p329-344-11">[11]</a></sup> He was tried by the Inquisition, found "vehemently suspect of heresy", and forced to recant. He spent the rest of his life under house arrest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> While under house arrest, he wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Two_New_Sciences" title="Two New Sciences">Two New Sciences</a></i>, in which he summarized work he had done some forty years earlier on the two sciences now called <a href="/wiki/Kinematics" title="Kinematics">kinematics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Strength_of_materials" title="Strength of materials">strength of materials</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JoCarney_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-JoCarney-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allan-Olney_1870_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allan-Olney_1870-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>

<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Mathematics"><span class="tocnumber">4.5</span> <span class="toctext">Mathematics</span></a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Timeline"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Timeline</span></a></li>

</li>

</ul>

</ul>
</li>
</ul>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_family">Early life and family</span></h2>
<p>Galileo was born in <a href="/wiki/Pisa" title="Pisa">Pisa</a> (then part of the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Florence" title="Duchy of Florence">Duchy of Florence</a>), Italy, on 15 February 1564,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> the first of six children of <a href="/wiki/Vincenzo_Galilei" title="Vincenzo Galilei">Vincenzo Galilei</a>, a famous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lutenist" title="Lutenist">lutenist</a>, composer, and <a href="/wiki/Music_theory" title="Music theory">music theorist</a>, and Giulia (née Ammannati), who had married in 1562. Galileo became an accomplished lutenist himself and would have learned early from his father a scepticism for established authority,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> the value of well-measured or quantified experimentation, an appreciation for a periodic or musical measure of time or rhythm, as well as the results expected from a combination of mathematics and experiment.
</p><p>Three of Galileo's five siblings survived infancy. The youngest, <a href="/wiki/Michelagnolo_Galilei" title="Michelagnolo Galilei">Michelangelo</a> (or Michelagnolo), also became a noted lutenist and composer although he contributed to financial burdens during Galileo's young adulthood. Michelangelo was unable to contribute his fair share of their father's promised dowries to their brothers-in-law, who would later attempt to seek legal remedies for payments due. Michelangelo would also occasionally have to borrow funds from Galileo to support his musical endeavours and excursions. These financial burdens may have contributed to Galileo's early desire to develop inventions that would bring him additional income.
</p><p>When Galileo Galilei was eight, his family moved to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Florence,_Italy" title="Florence, Italy">Florence</a>, but he was left with Jacopo Borghini for two years. He was educated from 1575 to 1578 in the  <a href="/wiki/Vallombrosa_Abbey" title="Vallombrosa Abbey">Vallombrosa Abbey</a>, about 30 km southeast of Florence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span></h3>
<p>The surname Galilei derives from the given name of an ancestor, Galileo Bonaiuti, a physician, university teacher and politician who lived in Florence from 1370 to 1450; his descendants had changed their family name from Bonaiuti (or Buonaiuti) to Galilei in his honor in the late 14th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Galileo Bonaiuti was buried in the same church, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Santa_Croce,_Florence" title="Basilica of Santa Croce, Florence">Basilica of Santa Croce in Florence</a>, where about 200 years later his more famous descendant Galileo Galilei was also buried.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>It was common for mid-sixteenth-century Tuscan families to name the eldest son after the parents' surname.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Hence, Galileo Galilei was not necessarily named after his ancestor Galileo Bonaiuti. The Italian male given name "Galileo" (and thence the surname "Galilei") derives from the Latin "Galilaeus", meaning "of <a href="/wiki/Galilee" title="Galilee">Galilee</a>", a biblically significant region in Northern <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Because of that region, the adjective <i>galilaios</i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <i>γαλιλαίος</i>, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>Galilaeus</i>, <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> <i>galileo</i>), which means "Galilean", has been used in antiquity (particularly by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperor_Julian" title="Emperor Julian">emperor Julian</a>) to refer to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christ" title="Christ">Christ</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">his followers</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> after this, the adjective has been adopted as a name with a meaning similar to <i><a href="/wiki/Jesus_(name)" title="Jesus (name)">Jesus</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Christian_(given_name)" title="Christian (given name)">Christian</a></i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The biblical roots of Galileo's name and surname were to become the subject of a famous pun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> In 1614, during the <a href="/wiki/Galileo_affair" title="Galileo affair">Galileo affair</a>, one of Galileo's opponents, the Dominican priest <a href="/wiki/Tommaso_Caccini" title="Tommaso Caccini">Tommaso Caccini</a>, delivered against Galileo a controversial and influential <a href="/wiki/Tommaso_Caccini#Caccini's_Sermon_at_Santa_Maria_Novella" title="Tommaso Caccini">sermon</a>. In it he made a point of quoting <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Acts of the Apostles">Acts</a> <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Acts+1%3A11&amp;version=KJV" rel="nofollow">1:11</a></span>, "Ye men of Galilee, why stand ye gazing up into heaven?".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naess_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-naess-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Children">Children</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Suor_maria_celeste.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="869" data-file-width="612" decoding="async" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Suor_maria_celeste.jpg/170px-Suor_maria_celeste.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Suor_maria_celeste.jpg/255px-Suor_maria_celeste.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Suor_maria_celeste.jpg/340px-Suor_maria_celeste.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Suor_maria_celeste.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Galileo's beloved elder daughter, Virginia (<a href="/wiki/Nun" title="Nun">Sister</a> <a href="/wiki/Maria_Celeste" title="Maria Celeste">Maria Celeste</a>), was particularly devoted to her father. She is buried with him in his tomb in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Santa_Croce,_Florence" title="Basilica of Santa Croce, Florence">Basilica of Santa Croce, Florence</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Despite being a genuinely pious Roman Catholic,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Galileo fathered three children out of wedlock with <a href="/wiki/Marina_Gamba" title="Marina Gamba">Marina Gamba</a>. They had two daughters, Virginia (born in 1600) and Livia (born in 1601), and a son, <a href="/wiki/Vincenzo_Gamba" title="Vincenzo Gamba">Vincenzo</a> (born in 1606).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RosenGothard2009_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-RosenGothard2009-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of their illegitimate birth, their father considered the girls unmarriageable, if not posing problems of prohibitively expensive support or dowries, which would have been similar to Galileo's previous extensive financial problems with two of his sisters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Their only worthy alternative was the religious life. Both girls were accepted by the <a class="extiw" href="https://it.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chiesa_di_San_Matteo_in_Arcetri" title="it:Chiesa di San Matteo in Arcetri">convent of San Matteo</a> in <a href="/wiki/Arcetri" title="Arcetri">Arcetri</a> and remained there for the rest of their lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-daughters_unmarriageable_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-daughters_unmarriageable-32">[32]</a></sup> Virginia took the name <a href="/wiki/Maria_Celeste" title="Maria Celeste">Maria Celeste</a> upon entering the convent. She died on 2 April 1634, and is buried with Galileo at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Santa_Croce,_Florence" title="Basilica of Santa Croce, Florence">Basilica of Santa Croce, Florence</a>. Livia took the name Sister Arcangela and was ill for most of her life. Vincenzo was later <a href="/wiki/Legitimation" title="Legitimation">legitimised</a> as the legal heir of Galileo and married Sestilia Bocchineri.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career_as_a_scientist">Career as a scientist</span></h2>
<p>Although Galileo seriously considered the priesthood as a young man, at his father's urging he instead enrolled in 1580 at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pisa" title="University of Pisa">University of Pisa</a> for a medical degree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> In 1581, when he was studying medicine, he noticed a swinging <a href="/wiki/Chandelier" title="Chandelier">chandelier</a>, which air currents shifted about to swing in larger and smaller arcs. To him, it seemed, by comparison with his heartbeat, that the chandelier took the same amount of time to swing back and forth, no matter how far it was swinging. When he returned home, he set up two <a href="/wiki/Pendulum" title="Pendulum">pendulums</a> of equal length and swung one with a large sweep and the other with a small sweep and found that they kept time together. It was not until the work of <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a>, almost one hundred years later, that the <a href="/wiki/Tautochrone_curve" title="Tautochrone curve">tautochrone</a> nature of a swinging pendulum was used to create an accurate timepiece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asimov_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Asimov-35">[35]</a></sup> Up to this point, Galileo had deliberately been kept away from mathematics, since a physician earned a higher income than a mathematician. However, after accidentally attending a lecture on geometry, he talked his reluctant father into letting him study mathematics and <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a> instead of medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asimov_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Asimov-35">[35]</a></sup> He created a <a href="/wiki/Thermoscope" title="Thermoscope">thermoscope</a>, a forerunner of the <a href="/wiki/Thermometer" title="Thermometer">thermometer</a>, and, in 1586, published a small book on the design of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrostatic" title="Hydrostatic">hydrostatic</a> balance he had invented (which first brought him to the attention of the scholarly world). Galileo also studied <i>disegno</i>, a term encompassing fine art, and, in 1588, obtained the position of instructor in the <a href="/wiki/Accademia_delle_Arti_del_Disegno" title="Accademia delle Arti del Disegno">Accademia delle Arti del Disegno</a> in Florence, teaching perspective and <a href="/wiki/Chiaroscuro" title="Chiaroscuro">chiaroscuro</a>. Being inspired by the artistic tradition of the city and the works of the Renaissance artists, Galileo acquired an <a href="/wiki/Aestheticism" title="Aestheticism">aesthetic mentality</a>. While a young teacher at the Accademia, he began a lifelong friendship with the Florentine painter <a href="/wiki/Cigoli" title="Cigoli">Cigoli</a>, who included Galileo's lunar observations in one of his paintings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-edgerton_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-edgerton-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-panofsky_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-panofsky-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1589, he was appointed to the chair of mathematics in Pisa. In 1591, his father died, and he was entrusted with the care of his younger brother <a href="/wiki/Michelagnolo_Galilei" title="Michelagnolo Galilei">Michelagnolo</a>. In 1592, he moved to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Padua" title="University of Padua">University of Padua</a> where he taught geometry, <a href="/wiki/Mechanics" title="Mechanics">mechanics</a>, and astronomy until 1610.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> During this period, Galileo made significant discoveries in both pure <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fundamental_science" title="Fundamental science">fundamental science</a> (for example, kinematics of motion and astronomy) as well as practical <a href="/wiki/Applied_science" title="Applied science">applied science</a> (for example, strength of materials and pioneering the telescope). His multiple interests included the study of <a href="/wiki/Astrology" title="Astrology">astrology</a>, which at the time was a discipline tied to the studies of mathematics and astronomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Galileo.2C_Kepler_and_theories_of_tides"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Galileo,_Kepler_and_theories_of_tides">Galileo, Kepler and theories of tides</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_Galilei_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="886" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Galileo_Galilei_2.jpg/180px-Galileo_Galilei_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Galileo_Galilei_2.jpg/270px-Galileo_Galilei_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Galileo_Galilei_2.jpg/360px-Galileo_Galilei_2.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_Galilei_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Galileo Galilei, portrait by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domenico_Robusti" title="Domenico Robusti">Tintoretto.</a></div></div></div>
<p>Cardinal Bellarmine had written in 1615 that the <a href="/wiki/Copernican_heliocentrism" title="Copernican heliocentrism">Copernican system</a> could not be defended without "a true <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_entity" title="Physical entity">physical</a> demonstration that the sun does not circle the earth but the earth circles the sun".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Galileo considered his theory of the tides to provide the required physical proof of the motion of the earth. This theory was so important to him that he originally intended to entitle his <i>Dialogue on the Two Chief World Systems</i> the <i>Dialogue on the Ebb and Flow of the Sea</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The reference to tides was removed from the title by order of the Inquisition.
</p><p>For Galileo, the <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tides</a> were caused by the sloshing back and forth of water in the seas as a point on the Earth's surface sped up and slowed down because of the Earth's rotation on its axis and revolution around the Sun. He circulated his first account of the tides in 1616, addressed to Cardinal Orsini.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> His theory gave the first insight into the importance of the shapes of ocean basins in the size and timing of tides; he correctly accounted, for instance, for the negligible tides halfway along the <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Sea" title="Adriatic Sea">Adriatic Sea</a> compared to those at the ends. As a general account of the cause of tides, however, his theory was a failure.
</p><p>If this theory were correct, there would be only one high tide per day. Galileo and his contemporaries were aware of this inadequacy because there are two daily high tides at <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a> instead of one, about twelve hours apart. Galileo dismissed this anomaly as the result of several secondary causes including the shape of the sea, its depth, and other factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Against the assertion that Galileo was deceptive in making these arguments, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> expressed the opinion that Galileo developed his "fascinating arguments" and accepted them uncritically out of a desire for physical proof of the motion of the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Galileo dismissed the idea, held by his contemporary <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a>, that the moon caused the tides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> (Galileo also took no interest in Kepler's elliptical orbits of the planets.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversy_over_comets_and_The_Assayer">Controversy over comets and <i>The Assayer</i></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Assayer" title="The Assayer">The Assayer</a></div>
<p>In 1619, Galileo became embroiled in a controversy with Father <a href="/wiki/Orazio_Grassi" title="Orazio Grassi">Orazio Grassi</a>, professor of mathematics at the Jesuit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collegio_Romano" title="Collegio Romano">Collegio Romano</a>. It began as a dispute over the nature of comets, but by the time Galileo had published <i><a href="/wiki/The_Assayer" title="The Assayer">The Assayer</a></i> (<i>Il Saggiatore</i>) in 1623, his last salvo in the dispute, it had become a much wider <a href="/wiki/Controversy" title="Controversy">controversy</a> over the very nature of <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> itself. The title page of the book describes Galileo as philosopher and "Matematico Primario" of the Grand Duke of Tuscany.
</p><p>Because <i>The Assayer</i> contains such a wealth of Galileo's ideas on how science should be practised, it has been referred to as his scientific manifesto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scientific_manifesto_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-scientific_manifesto-48">[48]</a></sup> Early in 1619, Father Grassi had anonymously published a pamphlet, <i>An Astronomical Disputation on the Three Comets of the Year 1618</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-disputatio_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-disputatio-49">[49]</a></sup> which discussed the nature of a comet that had appeared late in November of the previous year. Grassi concluded that the comet was a fiery body which had moved along a segment of a great circle at a constant distance from the earth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grassi_great_circle_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-grassi_great_circle-50">[50]</a></sup> and since it moved in the sky more slowly than the moon, it must be farther away than the moon.
</p><p>Grassi's arguments and conclusions were criticised in a subsequent article, <i><a href="/wiki/Discourse_on_Comets" title="Discourse on Comets">Discourse on Comets</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-discourse_on_comets_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-discourse_on_comets-51">[51]</a></sup> published under the name of one of Galileo's disciples, a Florentine lawyer named <a href="/wiki/Mario_Guiducci" title="Mario Guiducci">Mario Guiducci</a>, although it had been largely written by Galileo himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-authorship_of_discourse_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-authorship_of_discourse-52">[52]</a></sup> Galileo and Guiducci offered no definitive theory of their own on the nature of comets<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-criticism_of_previous_theories_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-criticism_of_previous_theories-53">[53]</a></sup> although they did present some tentative conjectures that are now known to be mistaken. In its opening passage, Galileo and Guiducci's <i>Discourse</i> gratuitously insulted the Jesuit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christopher_Scheiner" title="Christopher Scheiner">Christopher Scheiner</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scheiner_insult_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scheiner_insult-54">[54]</a></sup> and various uncomplimentary remarks about the professors of the Collegio Romano were scattered throughout the work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uncomplimentary_remark_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-uncomplimentary_remark-55">[55]</a></sup> The Jesuits were offended,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jesuits_offended_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-jesuits_offended-56">[56]</a></sup> and Grassi soon replied with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polemical" title="Polemical">polemical</a> tract of his own, <i>The Astronomical and Philosophical Balance</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-astronomical_balance_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-astronomical_balance-57">[57]</a></sup> under the pseudonym Lothario Sarsio Sigensano,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> purporting to be one of his own pupils.
</p><p><i>The Assayer</i> was Galileo's devastating reply to the <i>Astronomical Balance</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-the_assayer_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-the_assayer-59">[59]</a></sup> It has been widely recognized as a masterpiece of polemical literature,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-masterpiece_of_polemics_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-masterpiece_of_polemics-60">[60]</a></sup> in which "Sarsi's" arguments are subjected to withering scorn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-withering_scorn_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-withering_scorn-61">[61]</a></sup> It was greeted with wide acclaim, and particularly pleased the new pope, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urban_VIII" title="Urban VIII">Urban VIII</a>, to whom it had been dedicated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-assayer_success_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-assayer_success-62">[62]</a></sup> In Rome, in the previous decade, Barberini, the future Urban VIII, had come down on the side of Galileo and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lincean_Academy" title="Lincean Academy">Lincean Academy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Galileo's dispute with Grassi permanently alienated many of the Jesuits who had previously been sympathetic to his ideas,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jesuit_alienation_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-jesuit_alienation-64">[64]</a></sup> and Galileo and his friends were convinced that these Jesuits were responsible for bringing about his later condemnation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jesuits_responsible_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-jesuits_responsible-65">[65]</a></sup> The evidence for this is at best equivocal, however.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evidence_of_jesuits_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-evidence_of_jesuits-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversy_over_heliocentrism">Controversy over heliocentrism</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Galileo_affair" title="Galileo affair">Galileo affair</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_facing_the_Roman_Inquisition.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="920" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Galileo_facing_the_Roman_Inquisition.jpg/220px-Galileo_facing_the_Roman_Inquisition.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Galileo_facing_the_Roman_Inquisition.jpg/330px-Galileo_facing_the_Roman_Inquisition.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Galileo_facing_the_Roman_Inquisition.jpg/440px-Galileo_facing_the_Roman_Inquisition.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_facing_the_Roman_Inquisition.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cristiano_Banti" title="Cristiano Banti">Cristiano Banti</a>'s 1857 painting <i>Galileo facing the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Inquisition" title="Roman Inquisition">Roman Inquisition</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>In the whole world prior to Galileo's conflict with the Church, the majority of educated people subscribed either to the Aristotelian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geocentrism" title="Geocentrism">geocentric</a> view that the earth was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Center_of_the_Universe" title="History of the Center of the Universe">center of the universe</a> and that all heavenly bodies revolved around the Earth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> or the <a href="/wiki/Tychonic_system" title="Tychonic system">Tychonic system</a> that blended geocentrism with <a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">heliocentrism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hannam,_James_2011._p303-316_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hannam,_James_2011._p303-316-68">[68]</a></sup> Nevertheless, following the death of Copernicus and before Galileo, heliocentrism was relatively uncontroversial;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hannam,_James_2011._p303-316_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hannam,_James_2011._p303-316-68">[68]</a></sup> Copernicus's work was used by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_XIII" title="Pope Gregory XIII">Pope Gregory XIII</a> to reform the calendar in 1582.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[a]</a></sup>
</p><p>Opposition to heliocentrism and Galileo's writings combined religious and scientific objections. Scientific opposition came from <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a> and others and arose from the fact that, if heliocentrism were true, an annual <a href="/wiki/Stellar_parallax" title="Stellar parallax">stellar parallax</a> should be observed, though none was. Copernicus and Aristarchus had correctly postulated that parallax was negligible because the stars were so distant. However, Brahe had countered that, since stars appeared to have measurable size, if the stars were that distant, they would be gigantic, and in fact far larger than the Sun or any other celestial body. In Brahe's system, by contrast, the stars were a little more distant than Saturn, and the Sun and stars were comparable in size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graney_and_Danielson,_2014_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graney_and_Danielson,_2014-70">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Religious opposition to heliocentrism arose from Biblical references such as <a href="/wiki/Psalms" title="Psalms">Psalm</a> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Psalms#93:1" title="s:Bible (King James)/Psalms">93:1</a>, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/Psalms#96:10" title="s:Bible (King James)/Psalms">96:10</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Books_of_Chronicles" title="Books of Chronicles">1 Chronicles</a> <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Bible_(King_James)/1_Chronicles#16:30" title="s:Bible (King James)/1 Chronicles">16:30</a> which include text stating that "the world is firmly established, it cannot be moved." In the same manner, <a class="external text" href="https://www.biblica.com/bible/?osis=niv:Psalms.104:5–104:5" rel="nofollow">Psalm 104:5</a> says, "the Lord set the earth on its foundations; it can never be moved."  Further, <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastes" title="Ecclesiastes">Ecclesiastes</a> <a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/bibleversefinder2/index.php?book=Ecclesiastes&amp;verse=1:5–1:5&amp;src=1000">1:5</a> states that "And the sun rises and sets and returns to its place."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bellarmine_quote_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bellarmine_quote-71">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Galileo defended heliocentrism based on his astronomical observations of 1609 (<i><a href="/wiki/Sidereus_Nuncius" title="Sidereus Nuncius">Sidereus Nuncius</a></i> 1610). In December 1613, the Grand Duchess Christina of Florence confronted one of Galileo's friends and followers, <a href="/wiki/Benedetto_Castelli" title="Benedetto Castelli">Benedetto Castelli</a>, with biblical objections to the motion of the earth.  According to Maurice Finocchiaro, this was done in a friendly and gracious manner, out of curiosity. Prompted by this incident, Galileo wrote a <a href="/wiki/Letter_to_Benedetto_Castelli" title="Letter to Benedetto Castelli">letter to Castelli</a> in which he argued that heliocentrism was actually not contrary to biblical texts, and that the bible was an authority on faith and morals, not on science.  This letter was not published, but circulated widely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1615, Galileo's writings on heliocentrism had been submitted to the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Inquisition" title="Roman Inquisition">Roman Inquisition</a> by Father <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_Lorini" title="Niccolò Lorini">Niccolò Lorini</a>, who claimed that Galileo and his followers were attempting to reinterpret the Bible, which was seen as a violation of the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">Council of Trent</a> and looked dangerously like Protestantism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Langford_1992,_p.56-57_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Langford_1992,_p.56-57-73">[72]</a></sup> Lorini specifically cited Galileo's letter to Castelli.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup>  Galileo went to Rome to defend himself and his Copernican and biblical ideas. At the start of 1616, Monsignor <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Ingoli" title="Francesco Ingoli">Francesco Ingoli</a> initiated a debate with Galileo, sending him an essay disputing the Copernican system. Galileo later stated that he believed this essay to have been instrumental in the action against Copernicanism that followed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup>  According to Maurice Finocchiaro, Ingoli had probably been commissioned by the Inquisition to write an expert opinion on the controversy, and the essay provided the "chief direct basis" for the Inquisition's actions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup> The essay focused on eighteen physical and mathematical arguments against heliocentrism. It borrowed primarily from the arguments of Tycho Brahe, and it notedly mentioned Brahe's argument that heliocentrism required the stars to be much larger than the Sun. Ingoli wrote that the great distance to the stars in the heliocentric theory "clearly proves ... the fixed stars to be of such size, as they may surpass or equal the size of the orbit circle of the Earth itself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup>  The essay also included four theological arguments, but Ingoli suggested Galileo focus on the physical and mathematical arguments, and he did not mention Galileo's biblical ideas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> In February 1616, an Inquisitorial commission declared heliocentrism to be "foolish and absurd in philosophy, and formally heretical since it explicitly contradicts in many places the sense of Holy Scripture." The Inquisition found that the idea of the Earth's movement "receives the same judgement in philosophy and... in regard to theological truth it is at least erroneous in faith".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wcumf_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-wcumf-79">[78]</a></sup>  (The <a class="external text" href="https://arxiv.org/ftp/arxiv/papers/1402/1402.6168.pdf" rel="nofollow">original document</a> from the Inquisitorial commission was made widely available in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup>)
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_V" title="Pope Paul V">Pope Paul V</a> instructed Cardinal Bellarmine to deliver this finding to Galileo, and to order him to abandon the opinion that heliocentrism was physically true. On 26 February, Galileo was called to Bellarmine's residence and ordered:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p> ... to abandon completely... the opinion that the sun stands still at the center of the world and the earth moves, and henceforth not to hold, teach, or defend it in any way whatever, either orally or in writing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h218_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-h218-81">[80]</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>The decree of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congregation_of_the_Index" title="Congregation of the Index">Congregation of the Index</a> banned Copernicus's <i>De Revolutionibus</i> and other heliocentric works until correction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-h218_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-h218-81">[80]</a></sup> Bellarmine's instructions did not prohibit Galileo from discussing heliocentrism as a mathematical and philosophic idea, so long as he did not advocate for its physical truth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hannam,_James_2011._p329-344_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-Hannam,_James_2011._p329-344-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>For the next decade, Galileo stayed well away from the controversy. He revived his project of writing a book on the subject, encouraged by the election of Cardinal Maffeo <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barberini" title="Barberini">Barberini</a> as <a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_VIII" title="Pope Urban VIII">Pope Urban VIII</a> in 1623. Barberini was a friend and admirer of Galileo, and had opposed the condemnation of Galileo in 1616. Galileo's resulting book, <i><a href="/wiki/Dialogue_Concerning_the_Two_Chief_World_Systems" title="Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems">Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems</a></i>, was published in 1632, with formal authorization from the <a href="/wiki/Inquisition" title="Inquisition">Inquisition</a> and papal permission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pope_Urban_VIII_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pope_Urban_VIII-83">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Earlier, Pope Urban VIII had personally asked Galileo to give arguments for and against heliocentrism in the book, and to be careful not to advocate heliocentrism. He made another request, that his own views on the matter be included in Galileo's book. Only the latter of those requests was fulfilled by Galileo.
</p><p>Whether unknowingly or deliberately, Simplicio, the defender of the Aristotelian geocentric view in <i>Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems</i>, was often caught in his own errors and sometimes came across as a fool. Indeed, although Galileo states in the preface of his book that the character is named after a famous Aristotelian philosopher (<a href="/wiki/Simplicius_of_Cilicia" title="Simplicius of Cilicia">Simplicius</a> in Latin, "Simplicio" in Italian), the name "Simplicio" in Italian also has the connotation of "simpleton".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> This portrayal of Simplicio made <i>Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems</i> appear as an advocacy book: an attack on Aristotelian geocentrism and defence of the Copernican theory. Unfortunately for his relationship with the Pope, Galileo put the words of Urban VIII into the mouth of Simplicio.
</p><p>Most historians agree Galileo did not act out of malice and felt blindsided by the reaction to his book.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[b]</a></sup> However, the Pope did not take the suspected public ridicule lightly, nor the Copernican advocacy.
</p><p>Galileo had alienated one of his biggest and most powerful supporters, the Pope, and was called to Rome to defend his writings<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup> in September 1632. He finally arrived in February 1633 and was brought before inquisitor <a href="/wiki/Vincenzo_Maculani" title="Vincenzo Maculani">Vincenzo Maculani</a> to be <a href="/wiki/Criminal_charge" title="Criminal charge">charged</a>. Throughout his trial, Galileo steadfastly maintained that since 1616 he had faithfully kept his promise not to hold any of the condemned opinions, and initially he denied even defending them. However, he was eventually persuaded to admit that, contrary to his true intention, a reader of his <i>Dialogue</i> could well have obtained the impression that it was intended to be a defence of Copernicanism. In view of Galileo's rather implausible denial that he had ever held Copernican ideas after 1616 or ever intended to defend them in the <i>Dialogue</i>, his final interrogation, in July 1633, concluded with his being threatened with torture if he did not tell the truth, but he maintained his denial despite the threat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>The sentence of the Inquisition was delivered on 22 June. It was in three essential parts:
</p>
<ul><li>Galileo was found "vehemently suspect of heresy", namely of having held the opinions that the Sun lies motionless at the centre of the universe, that the Earth is not at its centre and moves, and that one may hold and defend an opinion as probable after it has been declared contrary to Holy Scripture. He was required to <a href="/wiki/Abjuration" title="Abjuration">"abjure, curse and detest"</a> those opinions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup></li>
<li>He was sentenced to formal imprisonment at the pleasure of the Inquisition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[87]</a></sup> On the following day, this was commuted to house arrest, which he remained under for the rest of his life.</li>
<li>His offending <i>Dialogue</i> was banned; and in an action not announced at the trial, publication of any of his works was forbidden, including any he might write in the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[88]</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:E_pur_si_muove.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6764" data-file-width="5738" decoding="async" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/E_pur_si_muove.jpg/220px-E_pur_si_muove.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/E_pur_si_muove.jpg/330px-E_pur_si_muove.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/E_pur_si_muove.jpg/440px-E_pur_si_muove.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:E_pur_si_muove.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait, attributed to Murillo, of Galileo gazing at the words "E pur si muove" (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E_pur_si_muove!" title="E pur si muove!">And yet it moves</a></i>) (not legible in this image) scratched on the wall of his prison cell</div></div></div>
<p>According to popular legend, after recanting his theory that the Earth moved around the Sun, Galileo allegedly muttered the rebellious phrase "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E_pur_si_muove!" title="E pur si muove!">And yet it moves</a>". A 1640s painting by the Spanish painter <a href="/wiki/Bartolom%C3%A9_Esteban_Murillo" title="Bartolomé Esteban Murillo">Bartolomé Esteban Murillo</a> or an artist of his school, in which the words were hidden until restoration work in 1911, depicts an imprisoned Galileo apparently gazing at the words "E pur si muove" written on the wall of his dungeon. The earliest known written account of the legend dates to a century after his death, but Stillman Drake writes "there is no doubt now that the famous words were already attributed to Galileo before his death".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>After a period with the friendly <a href="/wiki/Ascanio_II_Piccolomini" title="Ascanio II Piccolomini">Ascanio Piccolomini</a> (the Archbishop of <a href="/wiki/Siena" title="Siena">Siena</a>), Galileo was allowed to return to his villa at <a href="/wiki/Arcetri" title="Arcetri">Arcetri</a> near Florence in 1634, where he spent part of his life under house arrest. Galileo was ordered to read the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seven_Penitential_Psalms" title="Seven Penitential Psalms">seven penitential psalms</a> once a week for the next three years. However, his daughter Maria Celeste relieved him of the burden after securing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical" title="Ecclesiastical">ecclesiastical</a> permission to take it upon herself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>It was while Galileo was under house arrest that he dedicated his time to one of his finest works, <i><a href="/wiki/Two_New_Sciences" title="Two New Sciences">Two New Sciences</a></i>. Here he summarised work he had done some forty years earlier, on the two sciences now called <a href="/wiki/Kinematics" title="Kinematics">kinematics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Strength_of_materials" title="Strength of materials">strength of materials</a>, published in Holland to avoid the censor. This book has received high praise from <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[91]</a></sup>  As a result of this work, Galileo is often called the "father of modern physics". He went completely blind in 1638 and was suffering from a painful <a href="/wiki/Hernia" title="Hernia">hernia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Insomnia" title="Insomnia">insomnia</a>, so he was permitted to travel to Florence for medical advice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JoCarney_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-JoCarney-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allan-Olney_1870_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Allan-Olney_1870-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Dava_Sobel" title="Dava Sobel">Dava Sobel</a> argues that prior to Galileo's 1633 trial and judgement for heresy, Pope Urban VIII had become preoccupied with court intrigue and problems of state, and began to fear persecution or threats to his own life. In this context, Sobel argues that the problem of Galileo was presented to the pope by court insiders and enemies of Galileo. Having been accused of weakness in defending the church, Urban reacted against Galileo out of anger and fear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tomb_of_Galileo_Galilei.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="924" data-file-width="1231" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Tomb_of_Galileo_Galilei.JPG/220px-Tomb_of_Galileo_Galilei.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Tomb_of_Galileo_Galilei.JPG/330px-Tomb_of_Galileo_Galilei.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Tomb_of_Galileo_Galilei.JPG/440px-Tomb_of_Galileo_Galilei.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tomb_of_Galileo_Galilei.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tomb of Galileo, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basilica_di_Santa_Croce_di_Firenze" title="Basilica di Santa Croce di Firenze">Santa Croce</a>, Florence</div></div></div>
<p>Galileo continued to receive visitors until 1642, when, after suffering fever and heart palpitations, he died on 8 January 1642, aged 77.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JoCarney_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-JoCarney-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[93]</a></sup> The Grand Duke of Tuscany, <a href="/wiki/Ferdinando_II_de%27_Medici,_Grand_Duke_of_Tuscany" title="Ferdinando II de' Medici, Grand Duke of Tuscany">Ferdinando II</a>, wished to bury him in the main body of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basilica_di_Santa_Croce_di_Firenze" title="Basilica di Santa Croce di Firenze">Basilica of Santa Croce</a>, next to the tombs of his father and other ancestors, and to erect a marble mausoleum in his honour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-funeral_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-funeral-96">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:112px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dito_della_mano_destra_di_galileo,_in_teca_del_1737.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3228" data-file-width="1188" decoding="async" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Dito_della_mano_destra_di_galileo%2C_in_teca_del_1737.JPG/110px-Dito_della_mano_destra_di_galileo%2C_in_teca_del_1737.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Dito_della_mano_destra_di_galileo%2C_in_teca_del_1737.JPG/165px-Dito_della_mano_destra_di_galileo%2C_in_teca_del_1737.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Dito_della_mano_destra_di_galileo%2C_in_teca_del_1737.JPG/220px-Dito_della_mano_destra_di_galileo%2C_in_teca_del_1737.JPG 2x" width="110"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dito_della_mano_destra_di_galileo,_in_teca_del_1737.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Middle finger of Galileo's right hand</div></div></div>
<p>These plans were dropped, however, after Pope Urban VIII and his nephew, Cardinal Francesco Barberini, protested,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-funeral_protests_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-funeral_protests-97">[95]</a></sup> because Galileo had been condemned by the Catholic Church for "vehement suspicion of heresy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[96]</a></sup> He was instead buried in a small room next to the novices' chapel at the end of a corridor from the southern transept of the basilica to the sacristy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burial_spot_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-burial_spot-99">[97]</a></sup> He was reburied in the main body of the basilica in 1737 after a monument had been erected there in his honour;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reburial_spot_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-reburial_spot-100">[98]</a></sup> during this move, three fingers and a tooth were removed from his remains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[99]</a></sup> One of these fingers, the middle finger from Galileo's right hand, is currently on exhibition at the <a href="/wiki/Museo_Galileo" title="Museo Galileo">Museo Galileo</a> in Florence, Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_contributions">Scientific contributions</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_methods">Scientific methods</span></h3>
<p>Galileo made original contributions to the science of motion through an innovative combination of experiment and mathematics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup> More typical of science at the time were the qualitative studies of <a href="/wiki/William_Gilbert_(astronomer)" title="William Gilbert (astronomer)">William Gilbert</a>, on magnetism and electricity. Galileo's father, <a href="/wiki/Vincenzo_Galilei" title="Vincenzo Galilei">Vincenzo Galilei</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Lute" title="Lute">lutenist</a> and music theorist, had performed experiments establishing perhaps the oldest known non-linear relation in physics: for a stretched string, the pitch varies as the square root of the tension.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[102]</a></sup> These observations lay within the framework of the <a href="/wiki/Pythagoras#Musical_theories_and_investigations" title="Pythagoras">Pythagorean</a> tradition of music, well-known to instrument makers, which included the fact that subdividing a string by a whole number produces a harmonious scale. Thus, a limited amount of mathematics had long related music and physical science, and young Galileo could see his own father's observations expand on that tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Galileo was one of the first modern thinkers to clearly state that the <a href="/wiki/Physical_law" title="Physical law">laws of nature</a> are mathematical. In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Assayer" title="The Assayer">The Assayer</a></i>, he wrote "Philosophy is written in this grand book, the universe ... It is written in the language of mathematics, and its characters are triangles, circles, and other geometric figures;...."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[104]</a></sup> His mathematical analyses are a further development of a tradition employed by late scholastic natural philosophers, which Galileo learned when he studied philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[105]</a></sup> His work marked another step towards the eventual separation of science from both philosophy and religion; a major development in human thought. He was often willing to change his views in accordance with observation. In order to perform his experiments, Galileo had to set up standards of length and time, so that measurements made on different days and in different laboratories could be compared in a reproducible fashion. This provided a reliable foundation on which to confirm mathematical laws using <a href="/wiki/Inductive_reasoning" title="Inductive reasoning">inductive reasoning</a>.
</p><p>Galileo showed a modern appreciation for the proper relationship between mathematics, theoretical physics, and experimental physics. He understood the <a href="/wiki/Parabola" title="Parabola">parabola</a>, both in terms of <a href="/wiki/Conic_section" title="Conic section">conic sections</a> and in terms of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordinate" title="Ordinate">ordinate</a> (y) varying as the square of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abscissa" title="Abscissa">abscissa</a> (x). Galilei further asserted that the parabola was the theoretically ideal <a href="/wiki/Trajectory" title="Trajectory">trajectory</a> of a uniformly accelerated projectile in the absence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_resistance" title="Air resistance">air resistance</a> or other disturbances. He conceded that there are limits to the validity of this theory, noting on theoretical grounds that a projectile trajectory of a size comparable to that of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> could not possibly be a parabola,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[106]</a></sup> but he nevertheless maintained that for distances up to the range of the artillery of his day, the deviation of a projectile's trajectory from a parabola would be only very slight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy">Astronomy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bertini_fresco_of_Galileo_Galilei_and_Doge_of_Venice.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="737" decoding="async" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Bertini_fresco_of_Galileo_Galilei_and_Doge_of_Venice.jpg/220px-Bertini_fresco_of_Galileo_Galilei_and_Doge_of_Venice.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Bertini_fresco_of_Galileo_Galilei_and_Doge_of_Venice.jpg/330px-Bertini_fresco_of_Galileo_Galilei_and_Doge_of_Venice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Bertini_fresco_of_Galileo_Galilei_and_Doge_of_Venice.jpg/440px-Bertini_fresco_of_Galileo_Galilei_and_Doge_of_Venice.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bertini_fresco_of_Galileo_Galilei_and_Doge_of_Venice.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Galileo showed the <a href="/wiki/Doge_of_Venice" title="Doge of Venice">Doge of Venice</a> how to use the telescope (fresco by <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Bertini" title="Giuseppe Bertini">Giuseppe Bertini</a>)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_manuscript.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="813" data-file-width="569" decoding="async" height="314" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Galileo_manuscript.png/220px-Galileo_manuscript.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Galileo_manuscript.png/330px-Galileo_manuscript.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Galileo_manuscript.png/440px-Galileo_manuscript.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_manuscript.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>It was on this page that Galileo first noted an observation of the <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>. This observation upset the notion that all celestial bodies must revolve around the Earth. Galileo published a full description in <i>Sidereus Nuncius</i> in March 1610</div></div></div> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phases-of-Venus.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Phases-of-Venus.svg/220px-Phases-of-Venus.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Phases-of-Venus.svg/330px-Phases-of-Venus.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Phases-of-Venus.svg/440px-Phases-of-Venus.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phases-of-Venus.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Phases_of_Venus" title="Phases of Venus">phases of Venus</a>, observed by Galileo in 1610</div></div></div>
<p>Based only on uncertain descriptions of the first practical telescope which <a href="/wiki/Hans_Lippershey" title="Hans Lippershey">Hans Lippershey</a> tried to patent in the Netherlands in 1608,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[108]</a></sup> Galileo, in the following year, made a telescope with about 3x magnification. He later made improved versions with up to about 30x magnification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[109]</a></sup> With a <a href="/wiki/Refracting_telescope#Galileo's_telescope" title="Refracting telescope">Galilean telescope</a>, the observer could see magnified, upright images on the Earth—it was what is commonly known as a terrestrial telescope or a spyglass. He could also use it to observe the sky; for a time he was one of those who could construct telescopes good enough for that purpose. On 25 August 1609, he demonstrated one of his early telescopes, with a magnification of about 8 or 9, to <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venetian</a> lawmakers. His telescopes were also a profitable sideline for Galileo, who sold them to merchants who found them useful both at sea and as items of trade. He published his initial telescopic astronomical observations in March 1610 in a brief treatise entitled <i><a href="/wiki/Sidereus_Nuncius" title="Sidereus Nuncius">Sidereus Nuncius</a></i> (<i>Starry Messenger</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Kepler.27s_supernova"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Kepler's_supernova">Kepler's supernova</span></h4>
<p>Tycho and others had observed the supernova of 1572. Ottavio Brenzoni's letter of 15 January 1605 to Galileo brought the 1572 supernova and the less bright nova of 1601 to Galileo's notice. Galileo observed and discussed <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_Supernova" title="Kepler's Supernova">Kepler's supernova</a> in 1604. Since these new stars displayed no detectable <a href="/wiki/Parallax#Diurnal_parallax" title="Parallax">diurnal parallax</a>, Galileo concluded that they were distant stars, and, therefore, disproved the Aristotelian belief in the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Immutability_of_the_heavens&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Immutability of the heavens (page does not exist)">immutability of the heavens</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Jupiter.27s_moons"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Jupiter's_moons">Jupiter's moons</span></h4>
<p>On 7 January 1610, Galileo observed with his telescope what he described at the time as "three fixed stars, totally invisible<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[c]</a></sup> by their smallness", all close to Jupiter, and lying on a straight line through it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jupiter's_moons_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-jupiter's_moons-115">[112]</a></sup> Observations on subsequent nights showed that the positions of these "stars" relative to Jupiter were changing in a way that would have been inexplicable if they had really been fixed stars. On 10 January, Galileo noted that one of them had disappeared, an observation which he attributed to its being hidden behind Jupiter. Within a few days, he concluded that they were <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbiting</a> Jupiter:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[113]</a></sup> he had discovered <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Jupiter" title="Moons of Jupiter">three of Jupiter's four largest moons</a>. He discovered the fourth on 13 January. Galileo named the group of four the <i>Medicean stars</i>, in honour of his future patron, <a href="/wiki/Cosimo_II_de%27_Medici,_Grand_Duke_of_Tuscany" title="Cosimo II de' Medici, Grand Duke of Tuscany">Cosimo II de' Medici, Grand Duke of Tuscany</a>, and Cosimo's three brothers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-medicean_stars_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-medicean_stars-117">[114]</a></sup> Later astronomers, however, renamed them <i><a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean satellites</a></i> in honour of their discoverer. These satellites were independently discovered by <a href="/wiki/Simon_Marius" title="Simon Marius">Simon Marius</a> on 8 January 1610 and are now called <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a> the names given by Marius in his <i>Mundus Iovialis</i> published in 1614.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>His observations of the satellites of Jupiter caused a revolution in astronomy: a planet with smaller planets orbiting it did not conform to the principles of <a href="/wiki/On_the_Heavens" title="On the Heavens">Aristotelian cosmology</a>, which held that all heavenly bodies should circle the Earth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[116]</a></sup> and many astronomers and philosophers initially refused to believe that Galileo could have discovered such a thing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scepticism_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-scepticism-120">[117]</a></sup> His observations were confirmed by the observatory of <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Clavius" title="Christopher Clavius">Christopher Clavius</a> and he received a hero's welcome when he visited Rome in 1611.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[118]</a></sup> Galileo continued to observe the satellites over the next eighteen months, and by mid-1611, he had obtained remarkably accurate estimates for their periods—a feat which <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Kepler</a> had believed impossible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-periods_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-periods-122">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Venus.2C_Saturn.2C_and_Neptune"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Venus,_Saturn,_and_Neptune">Venus, Saturn, and Neptune</span></h4>
<p>From September 1610, Galileo observed that <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> exhibited a full set of <a href="/wiki/Lunar_phase" title="Lunar phase">phases</a> similar to that of the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliocentric_model" title="Heliocentric model">heliocentric model</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> developed by Nicolaus Copernicus predicted that all phases would be visible since the orbit of Venus around the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> would cause its illuminated hemisphere to face the Earth when it was on the opposite side of the Sun and to face away from the Earth when it was on the Earth-side of the Sun. On the other hand, in <a href="/wiki/Geocentric_model#Claudius_Ptolemy" title="Geocentric model">Ptolemy's geocentric model</a> it was impossible for any of the planets' orbits to intersect the spherical shell carrying the Sun. Traditionally, the orbit of Venus was placed entirely on the near side of the Sun, where it could exhibit only crescent and new phases. It was, however, also possible to place it entirely on the far side of the Sun, where it could exhibit only gibbous and full phases. After Galileo's telescopic observations of the crescent, gibbous and full phases of Venus, the Ptolemaic model became untenable. Thus in the early 17th century, as a result of his discovery, the great majority of astronomers converted to one of the various geo-heliocentric planetary models,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[120]</a></sup> such as the Tychonic, Capellan and Extended Capellan models,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[d]</a></sup> each either with or without a daily rotating Earth. These all had the virtue of explaining the phases of Venus without the vice of the 'refutation' of full heliocentrism's prediction of stellar parallax. Galileo's discovery of the phases of Venus was thus arguably his most empirically practically influential contribution to the two-stage transition from full geocentrism to full heliocentrism via geo-heliocentrism.
</p><p>Galileo observed the planet <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, and at first mistook its rings for planets, thinking it was a three-bodied system. When he observed the planet later, Saturn's rings were directly oriented at Earth, causing him to think that two of the bodies had disappeared. The rings reappeared when he observed the planet in 1616, further confusing him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>Galileo also observed the planet <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> in 1612. It appears in his notebooks as one of many unremarkable dim stars. He did not realise that it was a planet, but he did note its motion relative to the stars before losing track of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sunspots">Sunspots</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_galilei,_telescopi_del_1609-10_ca..JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1470" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Galileo_galilei%2C_telescopi_del_1609-10_ca..JPG/220px-Galileo_galilei%2C_telescopi_del_1609-10_ca..JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Galileo_galilei%2C_telescopi_del_1609-10_ca..JPG/330px-Galileo_galilei%2C_telescopi_del_1609-10_ca..JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Galileo_galilei%2C_telescopi_del_1609-10_ca..JPG/440px-Galileo_galilei%2C_telescopi_del_1609-10_ca..JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_galilei,_telescopi_del_1609-10_ca..JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Galileo's "cannocchiali" <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescopes</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Museo_Galileo" title="Museo Galileo">Museo Galileo</a>, Florence</div></div></div>
<p>Galileo made naked-eye and telescopic studies of <a href="/wiki/Sunspot" title="Sunspot">sunspots</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[123]</a></sup> Their existence raised another difficulty with the unchanging perfection of the heavens as posited in orthodox Aristotelian celestial physics. An apparent annual variation in their trajectories, observed by  <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Sizzi" title="Francesco Sizzi">Francesco Sizzi</a> and others in 1612–1613,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[124]</a></sup> also provided a powerful argument against both the Ptolemaic system and the geoheliocentric system of <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[e]</a></sup> A dispute over claimed priority in the discovery of sunspots, and in their interpretation, led Galileo to a long and bitter feud with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesuit" title="Jesuit">Jesuit</a> <a href="/wiki/Christoph_Scheiner" title="Christoph Scheiner">Christoph Scheiner</a>.  In the middle was <a href="/wiki/Mark_Welser" title="Mark Welser">Mark Welser</a>, to whom Scheiner had announced his discovery, and who asked Galileo for his opinion.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In fact, there is little doubt that both of them were beaten by <a href="/wiki/David_Fabricius" title="David Fabricius">David Fabricius</a> and his son <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Fabricius" title="Johannes Fabricius">Johannes</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Moon">Moon</span></h4>
<p>Prior to Galileo's construction of his version of a telescope, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Harriot" title="Thomas Harriot">Thomas Harriot</a>, an English mathematician and explorer, had already used what he dubbed a "perspective tube" to observe the Moon. Reporting his observations, Harriot noted only "strange spottednesse" in the waning of the crescent, but was ignorant to the cause. Galileo, due in part to his artistic training<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-panofsky_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-panofsky-37">[37]</a></sup> and the knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Chiaroscuro" title="Chiaroscuro">chiaroscuro</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-edgerton_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-edgerton-36">[36]</a></sup> had understood the patterns of light and shadow were, in fact, topographical markers. While not being the only one to observe the Moon through a telescope, Galileo was the first to deduce the cause of the uneven waning as light occlusion from lunar mountains and <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">craters</a>. In his study, he also made topographical charts, estimating the heights of the mountains. The Moon was not what was long thought to have been a translucent and perfect sphere, as Aristotle claimed, and hardly the first "planet", an "eternal pearl to magnificently ascend into the heavenly empyrian", as put forth by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dante" title="Dante">Dante</a>. Galileo is sometimes credited with the discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Libration#Lunar_libration" title="Libration">lunar libration in latitude</a> in 1632,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[125]</a></sup> although <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Harriot" title="Thomas Harriot">Thomas Harriot</a> or <a href="/wiki/William_Gilbert_(astronomer)" title="William Gilbert (astronomer)">William Gilbert</a> might have done it before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Milky_Way_and_stars">Milky Way and stars</span></h4>
<p>Galileo observed the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>, previously believed to be <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">nebulous</a>, and found it to be a multitude of <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a> packed so densely that they appeared from Earth to be clouds. He located many other stars too distant to be visible with the naked eye. He observed the double star <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mizar_(star)" title="Mizar (star)">Mizar</a> in Ursa Major in 1617.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[127]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <i>Starry Messenger</i>, Galileo reported that stars appeared as mere blazes of light, essentially unaltered in appearance by the telescope, and contrasted them to planets, which the telescope revealed to be discs. But shortly thereafter, in his <i><a href="/wiki/Letters_on_Sunspots" title="Letters on Sunspots">Letters on Sunspots</a></i>, he reported that the telescope revealed the shapes of both stars and planets to be "quite round". From that point forward, he continued to report that telescopes showed the roundness of stars, and that stars seen through the telescope measured a few seconds of arc in diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[128]</a></sup> He also devised a method for measuring the apparent size of a star without a telescope.
