As described in his <i>Dialogue Concerning the two Chief World Systems</i>, his method was to hang a thin rope in his line of sight to the star and measure the maximum distance from which it would wholly obscure the star. From his measurements of this distance and of the width of the rope, he could calculate the angle subtended by the star at his viewing point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[129]</a></sup> In his <i>Dialogue</i>, he reported that he had found the apparent diameter of a star of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stellar_magnitude" title="Stellar magnitude">first magnitude</a> to be no more than 5 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arcsecond" title="Arcsecond">arcseconds</a>, and that of one of sixth magnitude to be about <sup>5</sup>/<sub>6</sub> arcseconds. Like most astronomers of his day, Galileo did not recognise that the apparent sizes of stars that he measured were spurious, caused by diffraction and atmospheric distortion (see <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_seeing#The_effects_of_astronomical_seeing" title="Astronomical seeing">seeing disk</a> or <a href="/wiki/Airy_disk" title="Airy disk">Airy disk</a>), and did not represent the true sizes of stars. However, Galileo's values were much smaller than previous estimates of the apparent sizes of the brightest stars, such as those made by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tycho_brahe#Tycho's_geo-heliocentric_astronomy" title="Tycho brahe">Tycho Brahe</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Magnitude_(astronomy)" title="Magnitude (astronomy)">Magnitude</a>) and enabled Galileo to counter anti-Copernican arguments such as those made by Tycho that these stars would have to be absurdly large for their annual parallaxes to be undetectable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[130]</a></sup> Other astronomers such as <a href="/wiki/Simon_Marius" title="Simon Marius">Simon Marius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Battista_Riccioli" title="Giovanni Battista Riccioli">Giovanni Battista Riccioli</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Martin_van_den_Hove" title="Martin van den Hove">Martinus Hortensius</a> made similar measurements of stars, and Marius and Riccioli concluded the smaller sizes were not small enough to answer Tycho's argument.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Engineering">Engineering</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo%27s_geometrical_and_military_compass_in_Putnam_Gallery,_2009-11-24.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1955" data-file-width="3473" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Galileo%27s_geometrical_and_military_compass_in_Putnam_Gallery%2C_2009-11-24.jpg/220px-Galileo%27s_geometrical_and_military_compass_in_Putnam_Gallery%2C_2009-11-24.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Galileo%27s_geometrical_and_military_compass_in_Putnam_Gallery%2C_2009-11-24.jpg/330px-Galileo%27s_geometrical_and_military_compass_in_Putnam_Gallery%2C_2009-11-24.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Galileo%27s_geometrical_and_military_compass_in_Putnam_Gallery%2C_2009-11-24.jpg/440px-Galileo%27s_geometrical_and_military_compass_in_Putnam_Gallery%2C_2009-11-24.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo%27s_geometrical_and_military_compass_in_Putnam_Gallery,_2009-11-24.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Galileo's <a href="/wiki/Sector_(instrument)" title="Sector (instrument)">geometrical and military compass</a>, thought to have been made c. 1604 by his personal instrument-maker <a href="/wiki/Marc%27Antonio_Mazzoleni" title="Marc'Antonio Mazzoleni">Marc'Antonio Mazzoleni</a></div></div></div>
<p>Galileo made a number of contributions to what is now known as <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a>, as distinct from pure <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>. Between 1595 and 1598, Galileo devised and improved a <a href="/wiki/Sector_(instrument)" title="Sector (instrument)">geometric and military compass</a> suitable for use by <a href="/wiki/Artillery" title="Artillery">gunners</a> and <a href="/wiki/Surveying" title="Surveying">surveyors</a>. This expanded on earlier instruments designed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_Tartaglia" title="Niccolò Tartaglia">Niccolò Tartaglia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guidobaldo_del_Monte" title="Guidobaldo del Monte">Guidobaldo del Monte</a>. For gunners, it offered, in addition to a new and safer way of elevating <a href="/wiki/Cannon" title="Cannon">cannons</a> accurately, a way of quickly computing the charge of <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a> for <a href="/wiki/Round_shot" title="Round shot">cannonballs</a> of different sizes and materials. As a geometric instrument, it enabled the construction of any regular <a href="/wiki/Polygon" title="Polygon">polygon</a>, computation of the area of any polygon or circular sector, and a variety of other calculations. Under Galileo's direction, instrument maker <a href="/wiki/Marc%27Antonio_Mazzoleni" title="Marc'Antonio Mazzoleni">Marc'Antonio Mazzoleni</a> produced more than 100 of these compasses, which Galileo sold (along with an instruction manual he wrote) for 50 lire and offered a course of instruction in the use of the compasses for 120 lire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_temperature_and_pressure_measurement_technology" title="Timeline of temperature and pressure measurement technology">about 1593</a>, Galileo constructed a <a href="/wiki/Galileo_thermometer" title="Galileo thermometer">thermometer</a>, using the expansion and contraction of air in a bulb to move water in an attached tube.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_telescope_replica.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Galileo_telescope_replica.jpg/220px-Galileo_telescope_replica.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Galileo_telescope_replica.jpg/330px-Galileo_telescope_replica.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Galileo_telescope_replica.jpg/440px-Galileo_telescope_replica.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_telescope_replica.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A replica of the earliest surviving telescope attributed to Galileo Galilei, on display at the <a href="/wiki/Griffith_Observatory" title="Griffith Observatory">Griffith Observatory</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In 1609, Galileo was, along with Englishman <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Harriot" title="Thomas Harriot">Thomas Harriot</a> and others, among the first to use a <a href="/wiki/Refracting_telescope" title="Refracting telescope">refracting telescope</a> as an instrument to observe stars, planets or moons. The name "telescope" was coined for Galileo's instrument by a Greek mathematician, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Demisiani" title="Giovanni Demisiani">Giovanni Demisiani</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[133]</a></sup> at a banquet held in 1611 by Prince <a href="/wiki/Federico_Cesi" title="Federico Cesi">Federico Cesi</a> to make Galileo a member of his <a href="/wiki/Accademia_dei_Lincei" title="Accademia dei Lincei">Accademia dei Lincei</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[134]</a></sup> The name was derived from the Greek <i>tele</i> = 'far' and <i>skopein</i> = 'to look or see'. In 1610, he used a telescope at close range to magnify the parts of insects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telescope_microscope_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-telescope_microscope-140">[135]</a></sup> By 1624, Galileo had used<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-microscope_perfection_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-microscope_perfection-141">[136]</a></sup> a compound <a href="/wiki/Microscope" title="Microscope">microscope</a>. He gave one of these instruments to Cardinal Zollern in May of that year for presentation to the Duke of Bavaria,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zollern_microscope_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zollern_microscope-142">[137]</a></sup> and in September, he sent another to Prince Cesi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cesi_microscope_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cesi_microscope-143">[138]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Accademia_dei_Lincei" title="Accademia dei Lincei">Linceans</a> played a role again in naming the "microscope" a year later when fellow academy member <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Faber" title="Giovanni Faber">Giovanni Faber</a> coined the word for Galileo's invention from the Greek words <i>μικρόν</i> (<i>micron</i>) meaning "small", and <i>σκοπεῖν</i> (<i>skopein</i>) meaning "to look at". The word was meant to be analogous with "telescope".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[140]</a></sup> Illustrations of insects made using one of Galileo's microscopes and published in 1625, appear to have been the first clear documentation of the <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_microscope_technology" title="Timeline of microscope technology">use of a compound microscope</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-microscope_use_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-microscope_use-146">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1612, having determined the orbital periods of Jupiter's satellites, Galileo proposed that with sufficiently accurate knowledge of their orbits, one could use their positions as a universal clock, and this would make possible the determination of <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a>. He worked on this problem from time to time during the remainder of his life, but the practical problems were severe. The method was first successfully applied by <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Domenico_Cassini" title="Giovanni Domenico Cassini">Giovanni Domenico Cassini</a> in 1681 and was later used extensively for large land surveys; this method, for example, was used to survey France, and later by <a href="/wiki/Zebulon_Pike" title="Zebulon Pike">Zebulon Pike</a> of the midwestern United States in 1806. For sea navigation, where delicate telescopic observations were more difficult, the longitude problem eventually required development of a practical portable <a href="/wiki/Marine_chronometer" title="Marine chronometer">marine chronometer</a>, such as that of <a href="/wiki/John_Harrison" title="John Harrison">John Harrison</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[142]</a></sup> Late in his life, when totally blind, Galileo designed an <a href="/wiki/Escapement" title="Escapement">escapement</a> mechanism for a pendulum clock (called <a href="/wiki/Galileo%27s_escapement" title="Galileo's escapement">Galileo's escapement</a>), although no clock using this was built until after the first fully operational pendulum clock was made by <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a> in the 1650s.
</p><p>Galileo was invited on several occasions to advise on engineering schemes to alleviate river flooding.  In 1630 Mario Guiducci was probably instrumental in ensuring that he was consulted on a <a href="/wiki/Mario_Guiducci#Hydrology_of_the_Bisenzio_River" title="Mario Guiducci">scheme by Bartolotti</a> to cut a new channel for the <a href="/wiki/Bisenzio_(river)" title="Bisenzio (river)">Bisenzio River</a> near Florence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physics">Physics</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tito_Lessi_-_Galileo_and_Viviani.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="322" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Tito_Lessi_-_Galileo_and_Viviani.jpg/220px-Tito_Lessi_-_Galileo_and_Viviani.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Tito_Lessi_-_Galileo_and_Viviani.jpg/330px-Tito_Lessi_-_Galileo_and_Viviani.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/95/Tito_Lessi_-_Galileo_and_Viviani.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tito_Lessi_-_Galileo_and_Viviani.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Galileo e <a href="/wiki/Vincenzo_Viviani" title="Vincenzo Viviani">Viviani</a></i>, 1892, <a href="/wiki/Tito_Lessi" title="Tito Lessi">Tito Lessi</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pisa.Duomo.dome.Riminaldi01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1627" data-file-width="1515" decoding="async" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Pisa.Duomo.dome.Riminaldi01.jpg/220px-Pisa.Duomo.dome.Riminaldi01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Pisa.Duomo.dome.Riminaldi01.jpg/330px-Pisa.Duomo.dome.Riminaldi01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Pisa.Duomo.dome.Riminaldi01.jpg/440px-Pisa.Duomo.dome.Riminaldi01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pisa.Duomo.dome.Riminaldi01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dome of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Pisa" title="Cathedral of Pisa">Cathedral of Pisa</a> with the "lamp of Galileo"</div></div></div>
<p>Galileo's theoretical and experimental work on the motions of bodies, along with the largely independent work of Kepler and <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a>, was a precursor of the <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">classical mechanics</a> developed by <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Sir Isaac Newton</a>. Galileo conducted several experiments with <a href="/wiki/Pendulum" title="Pendulum">pendulums</a>. It is popularly believed (thanks to the biography by <a href="/wiki/Vincenzo_Viviani" title="Vincenzo Viviani">Vincenzo Viviani</a>) that these began by watching the swings of the bronze chandelier in the cathedral of Pisa, using his pulse as a timer. Later experiments are described in his <i><a href="/wiki/Two_New_Sciences" title="Two New Sciences">Two New Sciences</a></i>. Galileo claimed that a simple pendulum is <a href="/wiki/Pendulum#Period_of_oscillation" title="Pendulum">isochronous</a>, i.e. that its swings always take the same amount of time, independently of the <a href="/wiki/Amplitude" title="Amplitude">amplitude</a>. In fact, this is only approximately true,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[144]</a></sup> as was discovered by <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a>. Galileo also found that the square of the period varies directly with the length of the pendulum. Galileo's son, Vincenzo, sketched a clock based on his father's theories in 1642. The clock was never built and, because of the large swings required by its <a href="/wiki/Verge_escapement" title="Verge escapement">verge escapement</a>, would have been a poor timekeeper. (See <a href="#Engineering">Engineering</a> above.)
</p><p>Galileo is lesser known for, yet still credited with, being one of the first to understand sound frequency. By scraping a chisel at different speeds, he linked the pitch of the sound produced to the spacing of the chisel's skips, a measure of frequency. In 1638, Galileo described an experimental method to measure the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light#Measurement" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a> by arranging that two observers, each having lanterns equipped with shutters, observe each other's lanterns at some distance. The first observer opens the shutter of his lamp, and, the second, upon seeing the light, immediately opens the shutter of his own lantern. The time between the first observer's opening his shutter and seeing the light from the second observer's lamp indicates the time it takes light to travel back and forth between the two observers. Galileo reported that when he tried this at a distance of less than a mile, he was unable to determine whether or not the light appeared instantaneously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[145]</a></sup> Sometime between Galileo's death and 1667, the members of the Florentine <i><a href="/wiki/Accademia_del_Cimento" title="Accademia del Cimento">Accademia del Cimento</a></i> repeated the experiment over a distance of about a mile and obtained a similarly inconclusive result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[146]</a></sup> We now know that the speed of light is far too fast to be measured by such methods (with human shutter-openers on Earth).
</p><p>Galileo put forward <a href="/wiki/Galilean_invariance" title="Galilean invariance">the basic principle of relativity</a>, that the laws of physics are the same in any system that is moving at a constant speed in a straight line, regardless of its particular speed or direction. Hence, there is no absolute motion or absolute rest. This principle provided the basic framework for Newton's laws of motion and is central to <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Einstein's</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Special_theory_of_relativity" title="Special theory of relativity">special theory of relativity</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Falling_bodies">Falling bodies</span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Equations_for_a_falling_body" title="Equations for a falling body">Equations for a falling body</a></div>
<p>A biography by Galileo's pupil <a href="/wiki/Vincenzo_Viviani" title="Vincenzo Viviani">Vincenzo Viviani</a> stated that Galileo had <a href="/wiki/Galileo%27s_Leaning_Tower_of_Pisa_experiment" title="Galileo's Leaning Tower of Pisa experiment">dropped balls</a> of the same material, but different <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">masses</a>, from the <a href="/wiki/Leaning_Tower_of_Pisa" title="Leaning Tower of Pisa">Leaning Tower of Pisa</a> to demonstrate that their time of descent was independent of their mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[147]</a></sup> This was contrary to what Aristotle had taught: that heavy objects fall faster than lighter ones, in direct proportion to weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[148]</a></sup> While this story has been retold in popular accounts, there is no account by Galileo himself of such an experiment, and it is generally accepted by historians that it was at most a <a href="/wiki/Thought_experiment" title="Thought experiment">thought experiment</a> which did not actually take place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[150]</a></sup> An exception is Drake,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[151]</a></sup> who argues that the experiment did take place, more or less as Viviani described it. The experiment described was actually performed by <a href="/wiki/Simon_Stevin" title="Simon Stevin">Simon Stevin</a> (commonly known as Stevinus) and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jan_Cornets_de_Groot&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jan Cornets de Groot (page does not exist)">Jan Cornets de Groot</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asimov_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-Asimov-35">[35]</a></sup> although the building used was actually the church tower in Delft in 1586.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[152]</a></sup> However, most of his experiments with falling bodies were carried out using inclined planes where both the issues of timing and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_resistance" title="Air resistance">air resistance</a> were much reduced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:320px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Apollo_15_feather_and_hammer_drop.ogv/320px--Apollo_15_feather_and_hammer_drop.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:320px;height:240px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="48.848848848849" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Apollo_15_feather_and_hammer_drop.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/e/e8/Apollo_15_feather_and_hammer_drop.ogv/Apollo_15_feather_and_hammer_drop.ogv.240p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp9, opus&amp;quot;" data-title="Low bandwidth VP9 (180P)" data-shorttitle="VP9 180P" data-transcodekey="180p.vp9.webm" data-width="240" data-height="180" data-bandwidth="149552" data-framerate="29.97"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;'><img alt="File:Apollo 15 feather and hammer drop.ogv" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Apollo_15_feather_and_hammer_drop.ogv/220px--Apollo_15_feather_and_hammer_drop.ogv.jpg" style="width:220px;height:165px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e8/Apollo_15_feather_and_hammer_drop.ogv" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Apollo_15_feather_and_hammer_drop.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>During the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_15" title="Apollo 15">Apollo 15</a> mission in 1971, astronaut <a href="/wiki/David_Scott" title="David Scott">David Scott</a> showed that Galileo was right: acceleration is the same for all bodies subject to gravity on the Moon, even for a hammer and a feather.</div></div></div>
<p>In his 1638 <i>Discorsi</i>, Galileo's character Salviati, widely regarded as Galileo's spokesman, held that all unequal weights would fall with the same finite speed in a vacuum. But this had previously been proposed by <a href="/wiki/Lucretius" title="Lucretius">Lucretius</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[154]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Simon_Stevin" title="Simon Stevin">Simon Stevin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[155]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cristiano_Banti" title="Cristiano Banti">Cristiano Banti</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Filippo_Salviati" title="Filippo Salviati">Salviati</a> also held it could be experimentally demonstrated by the comparison of pendulum motions in air with bobs of lead and of cork which had different weight but which were otherwise similar.
</p><p>Galileo proposed that a falling body would fall with a uniform acceleration, as long as the resistance of the medium through which it was falling remained negligible, or in the limiting case of its falling through a vacuum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[156]</a></sup> He also derived the correct kinematical law for the distance travelled during a uniform acceleration starting from rest—namely, that it is proportional to the square of the elapsed time ( <i>d</i> ∝ <i>t</i><sup> 2</sup> ).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[157]</a></sup> Prior to Galileo, <a href="/wiki/Nicole_Oresme" title="Nicole Oresme">Nicole Oresme</a>, in the 14th century, had derived the times-squared law for uniformly accelerated change,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[158]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Domingo_de_Soto" title="Domingo de Soto">Domingo de Soto</a> had suggested in the 16th century that bodies falling through a homogeneous medium would be uniformly accelerated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[159]</a></sup> Galileo expressed the time-squared law using geometrical constructions and mathematically precise words, adhering to the standards of the day. (It remained for others to re-express the law in algebraic terms).
</p><p>He also concluded that objects <i>retain their velocity</i> in the absence of any impediments to their motion,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> thereby contradicting the generally accepted Aristotelian hypothesis that a body could only remain in so-called <a href="/wiki/Aristotelian_physics#Unnatural_motion" title="Aristotelian physics">"violent", "unnatural", or "forced" motion</a> so long as an agent of change (the "mover") continued to act on it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[160]</a></sup> Philosophical ideas relating to <a href="/wiki/Inertia" title="Inertia">inertia</a> had been proposed by <a href="/wiki/John_Philoponus" title="John Philoponus">John Philoponus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jean_Buridan" title="Jean Buridan">Jean Buridan</a>. Galileo stated: "Imagine any particle projected along a horizontal plane without friction; then we know, from what has been more fully explained in the preceding pages, that this particle will move along this same plane with a motion which is uniform and perpetual, provided the plane has no limits"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[161]</a></sup> This was incorporated into <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion" title="Newton's laws of motion">Newton's laws of motion</a> (first law).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematics">Mathematics</span></h3>
<p>While Galileo's application of mathematics to experimental physics was innovative, his mathematical methods were the standard ones of the day, including dozens of examples of an inverse proportion <a href="/wiki/Square_root" title="Square root">square root</a> method passed down from <a href="/wiki/Fibonacci" title="Fibonacci">Fibonacci</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archimedes" title="Archimedes">Archimedes</a>. The analysis and proofs relied heavily on the <a href="/wiki/Eudoxus_of_Cnidus#Mathematics" title="Eudoxus of Cnidus">Eudoxian theory of proportion</a>, as set forth in the fifth book of <a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_Elements" title="Euclid's Elements">Euclid's Elements</a>. This theory had become available only a century before, thanks to accurate translations by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_Tartaglia" title="Niccolò Tartaglia">Tartaglia</a> and others; but by the end of Galileo's life, it was being superseded by the algebraic methods of <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">Descartes</a>.
</p><p>The concept now named <a href="/wiki/Galileo%27s_paradox" title="Galileo's paradox">Galileo's paradox</a> was not original with him. His proposed solution, that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infinite_number" title="Infinite number">infinite numbers</a> cannot be compared, is no longer considered useful.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Timeline">Timeline</span></h2>

<ul><li>1543 – <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Nicolaus Copernicus</a> publishes <i><a href="/wiki/De_revolutionibus_orbium_coelestium" title="De revolutionibus orbium coelestium">De revolutionibus orbium coelestium</a></i> as an alternative world system to the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Geocentric_model" title="Geocentric model">geocentric model</a> causing subsequent questions to be raised about <a href="/wiki/Aristotelian_physics" title="Aristotelian physics">Aristotelian physics</a> following Copernicus' death</li>
<li>1563 – Parents <a href="/wiki/Vincenzo_Galilei" title="Vincenzo Galilei">Vincenzo Galilei</a> and Giulia Ammannati marry</li>
<li><b>1564 – Birth</b> in Pisa, Italy</li>
<li>~1570 – <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Digges" title="Thomas Digges">Thomas Digges</a> publishes <i>Pantometria</i> describing a telescope built between 1540–1559 by his father <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Digges_(scientist)" title="Leonard Digges (scientist)">Leonard Digges</a></li>
<li>1573 – <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a> publishes <i>De nova stella</i> (<i>On the new star</i>) refuting Aristotelian belief in immutable <a href="/wiki/Celestial_spheres" title="Celestial spheres">celestial spheres</a> and an eternal, unchanging, more perfect <a href="/wiki/Aristotelian_physics#celestial_unchanging" title="Aristotelian physics">heavenly realm</a> of celestial aether above the moon</li>
<li>1576 – <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Moletti" title="Giuseppe Moletti">Giuseppe Moletti</a>, Galileo's predecessor in the mathematics chair at Padua, reports falling bodies of the same shape fall at the same speed, regardless of material<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[162]</a></sup></li>
<li>1581 – His father, Vincenzo Galilei publishes <i>Dialogo della musica antica et moderna</i> formulating musical theories<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[163]</a></sup></li>
<li>1581 – Enrols as medical student at University of Pisa</li>
<li>1582 – Attends mathematics lecture by <a href="/wiki/Ostilio_Ricci" title="Ostilio Ricci">Ostilio Ricci</a> and decides to study math and science</li>
<li>1585 – Leaves <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pisa" title="University of Pisa">University of Pisa</a> without degree and works as tutor</li>
<li>1586 – Invents hydrostatic balance; wrote <i>La Balancitta</i> (<i>The little balance</i>)</li>
<li>1586 – <a href="/wiki/Simon_Stevin" title="Simon Stevin">Simon Stevin</a> publishes results for dropping lead weights from 10 meters</li>
<li>1588 – <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a> publishes work on comets containing a description of the <a href="/wiki/Tychonic_system" title="Tychonic system">Tychonic system</a> of the world<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[164]</a></sup></li>
<li>1589 – Appointed to Mathematics Chair, University of Pisa</li>
<li>~1590 – Partially completes <i><a href="/wiki/De_Motu_Antiquiora" title="De Motu Antiquiora">De Motu</a></i> (<i>On Motion</i>), which is never published</li>
<li>1591 – Death of his father, Vicenzo Galilei</li>
<li>1592 – Appointed professor of mathematics at University of Padua, remains 18 years</li>
<li>~1593 – Invents early thermometer that unfortunately depended on both temperature and pressure</li>
<li>~1595 – Invents improved ballistics calculation geometric and military compass, which he later improves for surveying and general calculations and earns income from tutoring on its use</li>
<li>1597 – Letter to <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Kepler</a> indicates his belief in the <a href="/wiki/Heliocentrism" title="Heliocentrism">Copernican System</a></li>
<li>1600 – First child, Virginia is born; ~1600 <i>Le Meccaniche</i> (<i>Mechanics</i>)</li>
<li>1600 – <a href="/wiki/William_Gilbert_(astronomer)" title="William Gilbert (astronomer)">William Gilbert</a> publishes <i><a href="/wiki/De_Magnete" title="De Magnete">On the Magnet and Magnetic Bodies, and on That Great Magnet the Earth</a></i> with arguments supporting the Copernican system</li>
<li>1600 – <a href="/wiki/Roman_Inquisition" title="Roman Inquisition">Roman Inquisition</a> finds <a href="/wiki/Giordano_Bruno" title="Giordano Bruno">Giordano Bruno</a>, Copernican system supporter, guilty of <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a> for opinions on <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">pantheism</a> and the eternal plurality of worlds, and for <a href="/wiki/Giordano_Bruno#Imprisonment,_trial_and_execution,_1593–1600" title="Giordano Bruno">denial</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Trinity" title="Holy Trinity">Trinity</a>, divinity of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christ" title="Christ">Christ</a>, virginity of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mary_(mother_of_Jesus)" title="Mary (mother of Jesus)">Mary</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Transubstantiation" title="Transubstantiation">Transubstantiation</a>; burned at the stake by civil authorities</li>
<li>1601 – Daughter Livia is born</li>
<li>1604 – Measures <a href="/wiki/Supernova#Observation_history" title="Supernova">supernova</a> position indicating no parallax for the new star</li>
<li>1605 – Sued by brothers-in-law for nonpayment of sisters' dowries</li>
<li>1606 – Son Vincenzo born</li>
<li>1606 – Publishes manual for his calculating compass</li>
<li>1607 – Rotilio Orlandini attempts to assassinate Galileo's friend, Friar <a href="/wiki/Paolo_Sarpi" title="Paolo Sarpi">Paolo Sarpi</a></li>
<li>1608 – <a href="/wiki/Hans_Lippershey" title="Hans Lippershey">Hans Lippershey</a> invents a <a href="/wiki/Refracting_telescope" title="Refracting telescope">refracting telescope</a></li>
<li>1609 – Independently invents and improves telescopes based on description of invention by Hans Lippershey</li>
<li>1609 – <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Kepler</a> publishes <i><a href="/wiki/Astronomia_nova" title="Astronomia nova">Astronomia nova</a></i> containing his first two <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion" title="Kepler's laws of planetary motion">laws</a> and for the first time demonstrates the Copernican model is more accurate than the Ptolemaic for uses such as navigation and prediction</li>
<li>1609 – <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Harriot" title="Thomas Harriot">Thomas Harriot</a> sketches the Moon from telescopic observations made four months before Galileo's</li>
<li>1610 – Publishes <i><a href="/wiki/Sidereus_Nuncius" title="Sidereus Nuncius">Sidereus Nuncius</a></i> (<i>Starry Messenger</i>); views our moon's mountains and craters and brightest 4 of <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Jupiter" title="Moons of Jupiter">Jupiter's moons</a></li>
<li>1610 – Martin Horky publishes Brevissima Peregrinatio Contra Nuncium Sidereum, opposing Galileo</li>
<li>1610 – Kepler requests one of Galileo's telescopes or lenses, but Galileo replies he is too busy to build one and has no extras<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[165]</a></sup></li>
<li>1610 – Lifetime appointment to mathematics position at University of Padua, and as mathematician and philosopher for <a href="/wiki/Cosimo_II_de%27_Medici,_Grand_Duke_of_Tuscany" title="Cosimo II de' Medici, Grand Duke of Tuscany">Cosimo II, Grand Duke of Tuscany</a></li>
<li>1611 – Discovers phases of Venus; granted audience with Pope; made member of <a href="/wiki/Accademia_dei_Lincei" title="Accademia dei Lincei">Lincean Academy</a></li>
<li>1611 – Awarded an honorary degree by the Jesuit College in Rome<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[166]</a></sup></li>
<li>1611 – <a href="/wiki/David_Fabricius" title="David Fabricius">David Fabricius</a> publishes <i>Narration on Spots Observed on the Sun and their Apparent Rotation with the Sun</i> prior to <a href="/wiki/Christoph_Scheiner" title="Christoph Scheiner">Christoph Scheiner</a> and Galileo's published works on the subject</li>
<li>1612 – Proposed Jupiter's moons could be used as a universal clock for possible determination of longitude</li>
<li>~1612 or 1613 – <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Sizzi" title="Francesco Sizzi">Francesco Sizzi</a> discovers annual variations in sunspots' motions</li>
<li>1613 – <i><a href="/wiki/Letters_on_Sunspots" title="Letters on Sunspots">Letters on Sunspots</a></i></li>
<li>1613 – Letter to <a href="/wiki/Benedetto_Castelli" title="Benedetto Castelli">Benedetto Castelli</a> discussing the rotation of the sun and Galileo's support of the Copernican system. Using <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inerrancy" title="Biblical inerrancy">Biblical inerrancy</a> as a basis, Galileo writes that <a href="/wiki/Joshua" title="Joshua">Joshua</a>'s command for the sun to stand still in Joshua 10:13 proves the "impossibility of the Aristotelian and Ptolemaic world system, and on the other hand agrees very well with the Copernican one."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castelli_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-Castelli-172">[167]</a></sup> He went on to write that "the sun gives not only light (as it obviously does) but also motion to all the planets that revolve around it" by <a href="/wiki/Solar_rotation" title="Solar rotation">its rotation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castelli_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-Castelli-172">[167]</a></sup></li>
<li>1615 – <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Letter_to_Grand_Duchess_Christina" title="Letter to Grand Duchess Christina">Letter to Grand Duchess Christina</a></i> (not published until 1636)</li>
<li>1616 – Officially warned by the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Church</a> not to <a href="/wiki/Galileo_affair" title="Galileo affair">hold or defend</a> the Copernican System</li>
<li>1616 – The <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> places <i><a href="/wiki/De_revolutionibus_orbium_coelestium#Reception" title="De revolutionibus orbium coelestium">De revolutionibus orbium coelestium</a></i> on the <a href="/wiki/Index_Librorum_Prohibitorum" title="Index Librorum Prohibitorum">List of Prohibited Books</a>, pending correction</li>
<li>1616 – Private letter "<a href="/wiki/Discourse_on_the_Tides" title="Discourse on the Tides">Discourse on the Tides</a>"</li>
<li>1617 – Moves into Bellosguardo, west of Florence, near his daughters' convent; observes double star Mizar in Ursa Major</li>
<li>1619 – <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Kepler</a> publishes <i><a href="/wiki/Harmonices_Mundi" title="Harmonices Mundi">Harmonices Mundi</a></i> which introduces his third <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion" title="Kepler's laws of planetary motion">law</a></li>
<li>1619 – <i>Discourse on the Comets</i></li>
<li>1623 – Maffeo Barberini becomes <a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_VIII" title="Pope Urban VIII">Pope Urban VIII</a></li>
<li>1623 – Publishes <i><a href="/wiki/The_Assayer" title="The Assayer">The Assayer</a></i></li>
<li>1624 – Visits Pope who praises and honours him, leaving with assumed permission to publish work on the Copernican vs. <a href="/wiki/Almagest" title="Almagest">Ptolemaic</a> Systems; used a compound microscope</li>
<li>1625 – Illustrations of insects made using one of Galileo's microscopes published</li>
<li>1630 – Completes <i>Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems</i> and subsequently receives approval of Church censor</li>
<li>1630 – Invited by <a href="/wiki/Ferdinando_II_de%27_Medici,_Grand_Duke_of_Tuscany" title="Ferdinando II de' Medici, Grand Duke of Tuscany">Grand Duke Ferdinand II of Tuscany</a> to advise on <a href="/wiki/Mario_Guiducci#Hydrology_of_the_Bisenzio_River" title="Mario Guiducci">proposed engineering works</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Bisenzio_(river)" title="Bisenzio (river)">Bisenzio River</a></li>
<li>1631 – Produces report on the Bisenzio engineering proposals, arguing against them</li>
<li>1632 – Publishes <i><a href="/wiki/Dialogue_Concerning_the_Two_Chief_World_Systems" title="Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems">Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems</a></i></li>
<li>1633 – <a href="/wiki/Galileo_affair" title="Galileo affair">sentenced by the Inquisition</a> to imprisonment, commuted to house arrest, for vehement suspicion of <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a> in violating the 1616 injunction</li>
<li>1633 – <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> places <i>Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems</i> on the <a href="/wiki/Index_Librorum_Prohibitorum" title="Index Librorum Prohibitorum">List of Prohibited Books</a></li>
<li>1638 – Publishes <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dialogues_Concerning_Two_New_Sciences" title="Dialogues Concerning Two New Sciences">Dialogues Concerning Two New Sciences</a></i></li>
<li><b>1642 – Death</b> in Arcetri, Italy</li>
<li>1668 – <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> builds <a href="/wiki/Newtonian_telescope" title="Newtonian telescope">his reflecting telescope</a></li>
<li>1687 – Newton publishes <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Philosophiæ Naturalis Principia Mathematica</a></i> deriving <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion" title="Kepler's laws of planetary motion">Kepler's laws</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation" title="Newton's law of universal gravitation">Universal Law of Gravitation</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion" title="Newton's laws of motion">Laws of Motion</a></li>
<li>2016 – The American <i><a href="/wiki/Juno_(spacecraft)" title="Juno (spacecraft)">Juno</a></i> spacecraft, carrying a plaque and a <a href="/wiki/Lego" title="Lego">Lego</a> minifigure dedicated at Galileo, arrives at Jupiter's orbit</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_Church_reassessments">Later Church reassessments</span></h3>
<p>The Galileo affair was largely forgotten after Galileo's death, and the controversy subsided. The Inquisition's ban on reprinting Galileo's works was lifted in 1718 when permission was granted to publish an edition of his works (excluding the condemned <i>Dialogue</i>) in Florence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-incomplete_works_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-incomplete_works-173">[168]</a></sup> In 1741, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Benedict_XIV" title="Pope Benedict XIV">Pope Benedict XIV</a> authorised the publication of an edition of Galileo's complete scientific works<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[f]</a></sup> which included a mildly censored version of the <i>Dialogue</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-complete_works_2_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-complete_works_2-175">[169]</a></sup> In 1758, the general prohibition against works advocating heliocentrism was removed from the <a href="/wiki/Index_Librorum_Prohibitorum" title="Index Librorum Prohibitorum">Index of prohibited books</a>, although the specific ban on uncensored versions of the <i>Dialogue</i> and Copernicus's <i>De Revolutionibus</i> remained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ban_not_lifted_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-ban_not_lifted-176">[170]</a></sup> All traces of official opposition to heliocentrism by the church disappeared in 1835 when these works were finally dropped from the Index.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ban_lifted_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-ban_lifted-177">[171]</a></sup>
</p><p>Interest in the Galileo affair was revived in the early 19th century, when Protestant polemicists used it (and other events such as the Spanish Inquisition and the <a href="/wiki/Myth_of_the_flat_Earth" title="Myth of the flat Earth">myth of the flat Earth</a>) to attack Roman Catholicism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hannam,_James_2011._p329-344_11-5"><a href="#cite_note-Hannam,_James_2011._p329-344-11">[11]</a></sup> Interest in it has waxed and waned ever since. In 1939, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_XII" title="Pope Pius XII">Pope Pius XII</a>, in his first speech to the Pontifical Academy of Sciences, within a few months of his election to the papacy, described Galileo as being among the "most audacious heroes of research... not afraid of the stumbling blocks and the risks on the way, nor fearful of the funereal monuments".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[172]</a></sup> His close advisor of 40 years, Professor Robert Leiber, wrote: "Pius XII was very careful not to close any doors (to science) prematurely. He was energetic on this point and regretted that in the case of Galileo."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[173]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 15 February 1990, in a speech delivered at the <a href="/wiki/Sapienza_University_of_Rome" title="Sapienza University of Rome">Sapienza University of Rome</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[g]</a></sup> Cardinal Ratzinger (later <a href="/wiki/Pope_Benedict_XVI" title="Pope Benedict XVI">Pope Benedict XVI</a>) cited some current views on the Galileo affair as forming what he called "a symptomatic case that permits us to see how deep the self-doubt of the modern age, of science and technology goes today".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-self-doubt_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-self-doubt-181">[174]</a></sup> Some of the views he cited were those of the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Paul_Feyerabend" title="Paul Feyerabend">Paul Feyerabend</a>, whom he quoted as saying "The Church at the time of Galileo kept much more closely to reason than did Galileo himself, and she took into consideration the ethical and social consequences of Galileo's teaching too. Her verdict against Galileo was rational and just and the revision of this verdict can be justified only on the grounds of what is politically opportune."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-self-doubt_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-self-doubt-181">[174]</a></sup> The Cardinal did not clearly indicate whether he agreed or disagreed with Feyerabend's assertions. He did, however, say "It would be foolish to construct an impulsive apologetic on the basis of such views."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-self-doubt_181-2"><a href="#cite_note-self-doubt-181">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 31 October 1992, <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_Paul_II" title="Pope John Paul II">Pope John Paul II</a> expressed regret for how the Galileo affair was handled, and issued a declaration acknowledging the errors committed by the Catholic Church tribunal that judged the scientific positions of Galileo Galilei, as the result of a study conducted by the <a href="/wiki/Pontifical_Council_for_Culture" title="Pontifical Council for Culture">Pontifical Council for Culture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[176]</a></sup> In March 2008, the head of the Pontifical Academy of Sciences, <a href="/wiki/Nicola_Cabibbo" title="Nicola Cabibbo">Nicola Cabibbo</a>, announced a plan to honour Galileo by erecting a statue of him inside the Vatican walls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[177]</a></sup> In December of the same year, during events to mark the 400th anniversary of Galileo's earliest telescopic observations, Pope Benedict XVI praised his contributions to astronomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[178]</a></sup> A month later, however, the head of the Pontifical Council for Culture, Gianfranco Ravasi, revealed that the plan to erect a statue of Galileo in the grounds of the Vatican had been suspended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[179]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_on_modern_science">Impact on modern science</span></h3>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hawking" title="Stephen Hawking">Stephen Hawking</a>, Galileo probably bears more of the responsibility for the birth of modern science than anybody else,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hawking_galileo_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hawking_galileo-187">[180]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> called him the father of modern science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-father_of_science_Einstein_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-father_of_science_Einstein-188">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[182]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2009_Austria_25_Euro_Year_of_Astronomy_Front.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="223" data-file-width="224" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fe/2009_Austria_25_Euro_Year_of_Astronomy_Front.jpg/170px-2009_Austria_25_Euro_Year_of_Astronomy_Front.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/f/fe/2009_Austria_25_Euro_Year_of_Astronomy_Front.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2009_Austria_25_Euro_Year_of_Astronomy_Front.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Euro_gold_and_silver_commemorative_coins_(Austria)#2009_Coinage" title="Euro gold and silver commemorative coins (Austria)">International Year of Astronomy commemorative coin</a></div></div></div>
<p>Galileo's astronomical discoveries and investigations into the Copernican theory have led to a lasting legacy which includes the categorisation of the four large moons of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> discovered by Galileo (<a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a> and <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>) as the <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons</a>. Other scientific endeavours and principles are named after Galileo including the <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">Galileo spacecraft</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[183]</a></sup> the first spacecraft to enter orbit around Jupiter, the proposed <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(satellite_navigation)" title="Galileo (satellite navigation)">Galileo</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_Navigation_Satellite_System" title="Global Navigation Satellite System">global satellite navigation system</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transformation_(geometry)" title="Transformation (geometry)">transformation</a> between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inertial_system" title="Inertial system">inertial systems</a> in <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">classical mechanics</a> denoted <a href="/wiki/Galilean_transformation" title="Galilean transformation">Galilean transformation</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gal_(unit)" title="Gal (unit)">Gal (unit)</a>, sometimes known as the <i>Galileo,</i> which is a non-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SI" title="SI">SI</a> unit of <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">acceleration</a>.
</p><p>Partly because 2009 was the fourth centenary of Galileo's first recorded astronomical observations with the telescope, the United Nations scheduled it to be the <a href="/wiki/International_Year_of_Astronomy" title="International Year of Astronomy">International Year of Astronomy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[184]</a></sup> A global scheme was laid out by the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> (IAU), also endorsed by <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a>—the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UN" title="UN">UN</a> body responsible for educational, scientific and cultural matters. The <a href="/wiki/International_Year_of_Astronomy" title="International Year of Astronomy">International Year of Astronomy</a> 2009 was intended to be a global celebration of astronomy and its contributions to society and culture, stimulating worldwide interest not only in astronomy but science in general, with a particular slant towards young people.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">Asteroid</a> <a href="/wiki/697_Galilea" title="697 Galilea">697 Galilea</a> is named in his honour.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_artistic_and_popular_media">In artistic and popular media</span></h3>
<p>Galileo is mentioned several times in the "opera" section of the <a href="/wiki/Queen_(band)" title="Queen (band)">Queen</a> song, "<a href="/wiki/Bohemian_Rhapsody" title="Bohemian Rhapsody">Bohemian Rhapsody</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[185]</a></sup> He features prominently in the song "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galileo_(Indigo_Girls_song)" title="Galileo (Indigo Girls song)">Galileo</a>" performed by the <a href="/wiki/Indigo_Girls" title="Indigo Girls">Indigo Girls</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amy_Grant" title="Amy Grant">Amy Grant</a>'s <i>Galileo</i> on her <i>Heart in Motion</i> album.
</p><p>Twentieth-century plays have been written on Galileo's life, including <i><a href="/wiki/Life_of_Galileo" title="Life of Galileo">Life of Galileo</a></i> (1943) by the German playwright <a href="/wiki/Bertolt_Brecht" title="Bertolt Brecht">Bertolt Brecht</a>, with a <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(1975_film)" title="Galileo (1975 film)">film adaptation</a> (1975) of it, and <i><a href="/wiki/Lamp_At_Midnight" title="Lamp At Midnight">Lamp At Midnight</a></i> (1947) by <a href="/wiki/Barrie_Stavis" title="Barrie Stavis">Barrie Stavis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[186]</a></sup> as well as the 2008 play "Galileo Galilei".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[187]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kim_Stanley_Robinson" title="Kim Stanley Robinson">Kim Stanley Robinson</a> wrote a science fiction novel entitled <i><a href="/wiki/Galileo%27s_Dream" title="Galileo's Dream">Galileo's Dream</a></i> (2009), in which Galileo is brought into the future to help resolve a crisis of scientific philosophy; the story moves back and forth between Galileo's own time and a hypothetical distant future and contains a great deal of biographical information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[188]</a></sup>
</p><p>Galileo Galilei was recently selected as a main motif for a high value collectors' coin: the €25 <a href="/wiki/Euro_gold_and_silver_commemorative_coins_(Austria)#2009_Coinage" title="Euro gold and silver commemorative coins (Austria)">International Year of Astronomy commemorative coin</a>, minted in 2009. This coin also commemorates the 400th anniversary of the invention of <a href="/wiki/Refracting_telescope#Galileo's_telescope" title="Refracting telescope">Galileo's telescope</a>. The obverse shows a portion of his portrait and his telescope. The background shows one of his first drawings of the surface of the moon. In the silver ring, other telescopes are depicted: the <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton_Telescope" title="Isaac Newton Telescope">Isaac Newton Telescope</a>, the observatory in <a href="/wiki/Kremsm%C3%BCnster_Abbey" title="Kremsmünster Abbey">Kremsmünster Abbey</a>, a modern telescope, a <a href="/wiki/Radio_telescope" title="Radio telescope">radio telescope</a> and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_observatory" title="Space observatory">space telescope</a>. In 2009, the <a href="/wiki/Galileoscope" title="Galileoscope">Galileoscope</a> was also released. This is a mass-produced, low-cost educational 2-inch (51 mm) telescope with relatively high quality.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writings">Writings</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_Galilei01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1280" data-file-width="711" decoding="async" height="306" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Galileo_Galilei01.jpg/170px-Galileo_Galilei01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Galileo_Galilei01.jpg/255px-Galileo_Galilei01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Galileo_Galilei01.jpg/340px-Galileo_Galilei01.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_Galilei01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Statue outside the <a href="/wiki/Uffizi" title="Uffizi">Uffizi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a></div></div></div>
<p>Galileo's early works describing scientific instruments include the 1586 tract entitled <i>The Little Balance</i> (<i>La Billancetta</i>) describing an accurate balance to weigh objects in air or water<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[189]</a></sup> and the 1606 printed manual <i>Le Operazioni del Compasso Geometrico et Militare</i> on the operation of a geometrical and military compass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>His early works in dynamics, the science of motion and mechanics were his <i>circa</i> 1590 Pisan <i><a href="/wiki/De_Motu_Antiquiora" title="De Motu Antiquiora">De Motu</a></i> (On Motion) and his <i>circa</i> 1600 Paduan <i>Le Meccaniche</i> (Mechanics). The former was based on Aristotelian–Archimedean fluid dynamics and held that the speed of gravitational fall in a fluid medium was proportional to the excess of a body's specific weight over that of the medium, whereby in a vacuum, bodies would fall with speeds in proportion to their specific weights. It also subscribed to the Philoponan <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Impetus_dynamics" title="Impetus dynamics">impetus dynamics</a> in which impetus is self-dissipating and free-fall in a vacuum would have an essential terminal speed according to specific weight after an initial period of acceleration.
</p><p>Galileo's 1610 <i><a href="/wiki/Sidereus_Nuncius" title="Sidereus Nuncius">The Starry Messenger</a></i> (<i>Sidereus Nuncius</i>) was the first scientific treatise to be published based on observations made through a telescope. It reported his discoveries of:
</p>
<ul><li>the <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons</a></li>
<li>the roughness of the Moon's surface</li>
<li>the existence of a large number of stars invisible to the naked eye, particularly those responsible for the appearance of the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a></li>
<li>differences between the appearances of the planets and those of the fixed stars—the former appearing as small discs, while the latter appeared as unmagnified points of light</li></ul>
<p>Galileo published a description of sunspots in 1613 entitled <i><a href="/wiki/Letters_on_Sunspots" title="Letters on Sunspots">Letters on Sunspots</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[191]</a></sup> suggesting the Sun and heavens are corruptible. The <i>Letters on Sunspots</i> also reported his 1610 telescopic observations of the full set of phases of Venus, and his discovery of the puzzling "appendages" of Saturn and their even more puzzling subsequent disappearance. In 1615, Galileo prepared a manuscript known as the "<a href="/wiki/Letter_to_the_Grand_Duchess_Christina" title="Letter to the Grand Duchess Christina">Letter to the Grand Duchess Christina</a>" which was not published in printed form until 1636. This letter was a revised version of the <i>Letter to Castelli</i>, which was denounced by the Inquisition as an incursion upon theology by advocating Copernicanism both as physically true and as consistent with Scripture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[192]</a></sup> In 1616, after the order by the inquisition for Galileo not to hold or defend the Copernican position, Galileo wrote the "<a href="/wiki/Discourse_on_the_Tides" title="Discourse on the Tides">Discourse on the Tides</a>" (<i>Discorso sul flusso e il reflusso del mare</i>) based on the Copernican earth, in the form of a private letter to <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Orsini_(cardinal)" title="Alessandro Orsini (cardinal)">Cardinal Orsini</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[193]</a></sup> In 1619, Mario Guiducci, a pupil of Galileo's, published a lecture written largely by Galileo under the title <i>Discourse on the Comets</i> (<i>Discorso Delle Comete</i>), arguing against the Jesuit interpretation of comets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[194]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1623, Galileo published <i><a href="/wiki/The_Assayer" title="The Assayer">The Assayer</a>—Il Saggiatore</i>, which attacked theories based on Aristotle's authority and promoted experimentation and the mathematical formulation of scientific ideas. The book was highly successful and even found support among the higher echelons of the Christian church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[195]</a></sup> Following the success of <i>The Assayer</i>, Galileo published the <i>Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems</i> (Dialogo sopra i due massimi sistemi del mondo) in 1632. Despite taking care to adhere to the Inquisition's 1616 instructions, the claims in the book favouring Copernican theory and a non Geocentric model of the solar system led to Galileo being tried and banned on publication. Despite the publication ban, Galileo published his <i><a href="/wiki/Two_New_Sciences" title="Two New Sciences">Discourses and Mathematical Demonstrations Relating to Two New Sciences</a></i> (<i>Discorsi e Dimostrazioni Matematiche, intorno a due nuove scienze</i>) in 1638 in <a href="/wiki/House_of_Elzevir" title="House of Elzevir">Holland</a>, outside the jurisdiction of the Inquisition.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Published_written_works">Published written works</span></h3>
<p>Galileo's main written works are as follows:
</p>
<ul><li><i>The Little Balance</i> (1586; in Italian: <i>La Billancetta</i>)</li>
<li><i>On Motion</i> (c. 1590; in Latin: <i><a href="/wiki/De_Motu_Antiquiora" title="De Motu Antiquiora">De Motu Antiquiora</a></i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[196]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Mechanics</i> (c. 1600; in Italian: <i>Le mecaniche</i>)</li>
<li><i>The Operations of Geometrical and Military Compass</i> (1606; in Italian: <i>Le operazioni del compasso geometrico et militare</i>)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Sidereus_Nuncius" title="Sidereus Nuncius">The Starry Messenger</a></i> (1610; in Latin: <i>Sidereus Nuncius</i>)</li>
<li><i>Discourse on Floating Bodies</i> (1612; in Italian: <i>Discorso intorno alle cose che stanno in su l'acqua, o che in quella si muovono</i>, "Discourse on Bodies that Stay Atop Water, or Move in It")</li>
<li><i>History and Demonstration Concerning Sunspots</i> (1613; in Italian: <i>Istoria e dimostrazioni intorno alle macchie solari</i>; work based on the <i>Three Letters on Sunspots</i>, <i>Tre lettere sulle macchie solari</i>, 1612)</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Letter_to_the_Grand_Duchess_Christina" title="Letter to the Grand Duchess Christina">Letter to the Grand Duchess Christina</a>" (1615; published in 1636)</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Discourse_on_the_Tides" title="Discourse on the Tides">Discourse on the Tides</a>" (1616; in Italian: <i>Discorso del flusso e reflusso del mare</i>)</li>
<li><i>Discourse on the Comets</i> (1619; in Italian: <i>Discorso delle Comete</i>)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Assayer" title="The Assayer">The Assayer</a></i> (1623; in Italian: <i>Il Saggiatore</i>)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Dialogue_Concerning_the_Two_Chief_World_Systems" title="Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems">Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems</a></i> (1632; in Italian: <i>Dialogo sopra i due massimi sistemi del mondo</i>)</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Two_New_Sciences" title="Two New Sciences">Discourses and Mathematical Demonstrations Relating to Two New Sciences</a></i> (1638; in Italian: <i>Discorsi e Dimostrazioni Matematiche, intorno a due nuove scienze</i>)</li></ul>

<li id="cite_note-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-69">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The Gregorian calendar, first adopted in 1582, was based on computations that made use of Copernicus' work" (<cite class="citation" id="CITEREFKuhn1957">Kuhn, Thomas (1957), <i>The Copernican Revolution</i>, Harvard University Press, p. 125</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Copernican+Revolution&amp;rft.pages=125&amp;rft.pub=Harvard+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1957&amp;rft.aulast=Kuhn&amp;rft.aufirst=Thomas&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGalileo+Galilei"></span><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r879151008">.mw-parser-output cite.citation{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .citation q{quotes:"\"""\"""'""'"}.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-free a{background:url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Lock-green.svg/9px-Lock-green.svg.png")no-repeat;background-position:right .1em center}.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-limited a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-registration a{background:url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Lock-gray-alt-2.svg/9px-Lock-gray-alt-2.svg.png")no-repeat;background-position:right .1em center}.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-subscription a{background:url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Lock-red-alt-2.svg/9px-Lock-red-alt-2.svg.png")no-repeat;background-position:right .1em a{background:url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/12px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png")no-repeat;background-position:right .1em center}.mw-parser-output code.cs1-code{color:inherit;background:inherit;border:inherit;padding:inherit}.mw-parser-output .cs1-hidden-error{display:none;font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-visible-error{font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-maint{display:none;color:#33aa33;margin-left:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .cs1-subscription,.mw-parser-output .cs1-registration,.mw-parser-output .cs1-format{font-size:95%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-left,.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-wl-left{padding-left:0.2em}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-right,.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-wl-right{padding-right:0.2em}</style>).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-85"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-85">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See Langford <a href="#Reference-Langford-1998">(1966, pp. 133–134)</a>, and Seeger <a href="#Reference-Seeger-1966">(1966, p. 30)</a>, for example. Drake <a href="#Reference-Drake-1978">(1978, p. 355)</a> asserts that Simplicio's character is modelled on the Aristotelian philosophers Lodovico delle Colombe and <a href="/wiki/Cesare_Cremonini_(philosopher)" title="Cesare Cremonini (philosopher)">Cesare Cremonini</a>, rather than Urban. He also considers that the demand for Galileo to include the Pope's argument in the <i>Dialogue</i> left him with no option but to put it in the mouth of Simplicio <a href="#Reference-Drake-1953">(Drake, 1953, p. 491)</a>. Even <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Koestler" title="Arthur Koestler">Arthur Koestler</a>, who is generally quite harsh on Galileo in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sleepwalkers_(Koestler_book)" title="The Sleepwalkers (Koestler book)">The Sleepwalkers</a></i> <a href="#Reference-Koestler-1990">(1959)</a>, after noting that Urban suspected Galileo of having intended Simplicio to be a caricature of him, says "this of course is untrue" <a href="#Reference-Koestler-1990">(1959, p. 483)</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-114"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-114">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>i.e.</i>, invisible to the naked eye.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-124"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-124">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the Capellan model only Mercury and Venus orbit the Sun, whilst in its extended version such as expounded by Riccioli, Mars also orbits the Sun, but the orbits of Jupiter and Saturn are centred on the Earth</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-129"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-129">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In geostatic systems the apparent annual variation in the motion of sunspots could only be explained as the result of an implausibly complicated precession of the Sun's axis of rotation <a href="#Reference-Linton-2004">(Linton, 2004, p. 212;</a> <a href="#Reference-Sharratt-1994">Sharratt</a>, 1994, p. 166; <a href="#Reference-Drake-1970">Drake, 1970, pp. 191–196)</a>. This did not apply, however, to the modified version of Tycho's system introduced by his protegé, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Longomontanus" title="Longomontanus">Longomontanus</a>, in which the Earth was assumed to rotate. Longomontanus's system could account for the apparent motions of sunspots just as well as the Copernican.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-174"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-174">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Two of his non-scientific works, the letters to Castelli and the Grand Duchess Christina, were explicitly not allowed to be included <a href="#Reference-Coyne-2005">(Coyne 2005, p. 347)</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-180"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-180">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">An earlier version had been delivered on 16 December 1989, in Rieti, and a later version in Madrid on 24 February 1990 <a href="#Reference-Ratzinger-1994">(Ratzinger, 1994, p. 81)</a>. According to Feyerabend himself, Ratzinger had also mentioned him "in support of" his own views in a speech in Parma around the same time <a href="#Reference-Feyerabend-1995">(Feyerabend, 1995, p. 178)</a>.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-88"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-88">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Reference-Fantoli-2005">Fantoli (2005, p. 139)</a>, <a href="#Reference-Finocchiaro-1989">Finocchiaro (1989, pp. 288–93)</a>. Finocchiaro's translation of the Inquisition's judgement against Galileo is <cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070930013053/http://astro.wcupa.edu/mgagne/ess362/resources/finocchiaro.html" rel="nofollow">"available on-line"</a>. Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://astro.wcupa.edu/mgagne/ess362/resources/finocchiaro.html" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on 30 September 2007<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2007-09-30</span></span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=available+on-line&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fastro.wcupa.edu%2Fmgagne%2Fess362%2Fresources%2Ffinocchiaro.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGalileo+Galilei"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r879151008" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>. "Vehemently suspect of heresy" was a technical term of <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">canon law</a> and did not necessarily imply that the Inquisition considered the opinions giving rise to the verdict to be heretical. The same verdict would have been possible even if the opinions had been subject to only the less serious censure of "erroneous in faith" <a href="#Reference-Fantoli-2005">(Fantoli, 2005, p. 140</a>; <a href="#Reference-Heilbron-2005">Heilbron, 2005, pp. 282–284)</a>.</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-163"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-163">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Reference-Clagett-1968">Clagett (1968, p. 561)</a>. Oresme, however, regarded this discovery as a purely intellectual exercise having no relevance to the description of any natural phenomena, and consequently failed to recognise any connection with the motion of falling bodies (<a href="#CITEREFGrant1996">Grant</a>, 1996, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=YyvmEyX6rZgC&amp;pg=PA103#v=onepage" rel="nofollow">p. 103</a>).</span>

<li id="cite_note-164"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-164">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Reference-Sharratt-1994">Sharratt (1994, p. 198)</a>, <a href="#Reference-Wallace-2004">Wallace (2004, pp.II 384, II 400, III 272)</a> Soto, however, did not anticipate many of the qualifications and refinements contained in Galileo's theory of falling bodies. He did not, for instance, recognise, as Galileo did, that a body would fall with a strictly uniform acceleration only in a vacuum, and that it would otherwise eventually reach a uniform terminal velocity.</span>

<li id="cite_note-Castelli-172"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Castelli_172-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Castelli_172-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="http://www.disf.org/en/documentation/03-Galileo_PBCastelli.asp" rel="nofollow">Galileo Galilei, "Letter to Benedetto Castelli (1613)"</a>, Religion and Science. (<a class="external text" href="http://www.law.umkc.edu/faculty/projects/ftrials/galileo/lettercastelli.html" rel="nofollow">Source of the English translation.</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20110206071010/http://www.law.umkc.edu/faculty/projects/ftrials/galileo/lettercastelli.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 6 February 2011 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-incomplete_works-173"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-incomplete_works_173-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Reference-Heilbron-2005">Heilbron (2005, p. 299)</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-complete_works_2-175"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-complete_works_2_175-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Reference-Heilbron-2005">Heilbron (2005, pp. 303–304)</a>; <a href="#Reference-Coyne-2005">Coyne (2005, p. 347)</a>. The uncensored version of the <i>Dialogue</i> remained on the Index of prohibited books, however <a href="#Reference-Heilbron-2005">(Heilbron 2005, p. 279)</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-ban_not_lifted-176"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-ban_not_lifted_176-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Reference-Heilbron-2005">Heilbron (2005, p. 307)</a>; <a href="#Reference-Coyne-2005">Coyne (2005, p. 347)</a> The practical effect of the ban in its later years seems to have been that clergy could publish discussions of heliocentric physics with a formal disclaimer assuring its hypothetical character and their obedience to the church decrees against motion of the earth: see for example the commented edition (1742) of Newton's 'Principia' by Fathers Le Seur and Jacquier, which contains such a disclaimer ('Declaratio') before the third book (Propositions 25 onwards) dealing with the lunar theory.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-ban_lifted-177"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-ban_lifted_177-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#Reference-McMullin-2005">McMullin (2005, p. 6)</a>; <a href="#Reference-Coyne-2005">Coyne (2005, p. 346)</a>. In fact, the Church's opposition had effectively ended in 1820 when a Catholic canon, Giuseppe Settele, was given permission to publish a work which treated heliocentrism as a physical fact rather than a mathematical fiction. The 1835 edition of the Index was the first to be issued after that year.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-178"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-178">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Discourse of His Holiness Pope Pius XII given on 3 December 1939 at the Solemn Audience granted to the Plenary Session of the Academy, Discourses of the Popes from Pius XI to John Paul II to the <a href="/wiki/Pontifical_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Pontifical Academy of Sciences">Pontifical Academy of the Sciences</a> 1939–1986, Vatican City, p. 34</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-179"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-179">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Robert Leiber, Pius XII Stimmen der Zeit, November 1958 in Pius XII. Sagt, Frankfurt 1959, p. 411</span>
</li>

</li>
